10095-8{_}1294{_}While he was earnestly cautioning Lucifer against this Cardinal as one who could and would cheat the very Devil himself, another key turned in the lock, and Benno escaped under the table, where Anno immediately inserted his finger into his right eye.
10095-8{_}1295{_}The little squeal consequent upon this occurrence Lucifer successfully smothered by a fit of coughing.
10095-8{_}1296{_}Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1297{_}3, a Frenchman, bore a Bayonne ham, and exhibited the same disgust as Benno on seeing himself forestalled.
10095-8{_}1298{_}So far as his requests transpired they were moderate, but no one knows where he would have stopped if he had not been scared by the advent of Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1299{_}4.
10095-8{_}1300{_}Up to this time he had only asked for an inexhaustible purse, power to call up the Devil _ad libitum_, and a ring of invisibility to allow him free access to his mistress, who was unfortunately a married woman.
10095-8{_}1301{_}Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1302{_}4 chiefly wanted to be put into the way of poisoning Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1303{_}5; and Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1304{_}5 preferred the same petition as respected Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1305{_}4.
10095-8{_}1306{_}Cardinal No.
10095-8{_}1307{_}6, an Englishman, demanded the reversion of the Archbishoprics of Canterbury and York, with the faculty of holding them together, and of unlimited non-residence.
10095-8{_}1308{_}In the course of his harangue he made use of the phrase _non obstantibus_, of which Lucifer immediately took a note.
10095-8{_}1309{_}What the seventh Cardinal would have solicited is not known, for he had hardly opened his mouth when the twelfth hour expired, and Lucifer, regaining his vigour with his shape, sent the Prince of the Church spinning to the other end of the room, and split the marble table with a single stroke of his tail.
10095-8{_}1310{_}The six crouched and huddling Cardinals cowered revealed to one another, and at the same time enjoyed the spectacle of his Holiness darting through the stone ceiling, which yielded like a film to his passage, and closed up afterwards as if nothing had happened.
10095-8{_}1311{_}After the first shock of dismay they unanimously rushed to the door,  but found it bolted on the outside.
10095-8{_}1312{_}There was no other exit, and no means of giving an alarm.
10095-8{_}1313{_}In this emergency the demeanour of the Italian Cardinals set a bright example to their ultramontane colleagues.
10095-8{_}1314{_}"_Bisogna pazienzia_," they said, as they shrugged their shoulders.
10095-8{_}1315{_}Nothing could exceed the mutual politeness of Cardinals Anno and Benno, unless that of the two who had sought to poison each other.
10095-8{_}1316{_}The Frenchman was held to have gravely derogated from good manners by alluding to this circumstance, which had reached his ears while he was under the table: and the Englishman swore so outrageously at the plight in which he found himself that the Italians then and there silently registered a vow that none of his nation should ever be Pope, a maxim which, with one exception, has been observed to this day.
10095-8{_}1317{_}Lucifer, meanwhile, had repaired to Silvester, whom he found arrayed in all the insignia of his dignity; of which, as he remarked, he thought his visitor had probably had enough.
10095-8{_}1318{_}"I should think so indeed," replied Lucifer.
10095-8{_}1319{_}"But at the same time I feel myself fully repaid for all I have undergone by the assurance of the loyalty of my friends and admirers, and the conviction that it is needless for me to devote any considerable amount of personal attention to ecclesiastical affairs.
10095-8{_}1320{_}I now claim the promised boon, which it will be in no way inconsistent with thy functions to grant, seeing that it is a work of mercy.
10095-8{_}1321{_}I demand that the Cardinals be released, and that their conspiracy against thee, by which I alone suffered, be buried in oblivion."
10095-8{_}1322{_}"I hoped you would carry them all off," said Gerbert, with an expression of disappointment.
10095-8{_}1323{_}"Thank you," said the Devil.
10095-8{_}1324{_}"It is more to my interest to leave them where they are."
10095-8{_}1325{_}So the dungeon-door was unbolted, and the Cardinals came forth, sheepish and crestfallen.
10095-8{_}1326{_}If, after all, they did less mischief than Lucifer had expected from them, the cause was their entire bewilderment by what had passed, and their utter inability to penetrate the policy of Gerbert, who henceforth devoted himself even with ostentation to good works.
10095-8{_}1327{_}They could never quite satisfy themselves whether they were speaking to the Pope or to the Devil, and when under the latter impression habitually emitted propositions which Gerbert justly stigmatised as rash, temerarious, and scandalous.
10095-8{_}1328{_}They plagued him with allusions to certain matters mentioned in their interviews with Lucifer, with which they naturally but erroneously supposed him to be conversant, and worried him by continual nods and titterings as they glanced at his nether extremities.
10095-8{_}1329{_}To abolish this nuisance, and at the same time silence sundry unpleasant rumours which had somehow got abroad, Gerbert devised the ceremony of kissing the Pope's feet, which, in a grievously mutilated form, endures to this day.
10095-8{_}1330{_}The stupefaction of the Cardinals on discovering that the Holy Father had lost his hoof surpasses all description, and they went to their graves without having obtained the least insight into the mystery.
10095-8{_}1331{_}THE CUPBEARER   The minister Photinius had fallen, to the joy of Constantinople.
10095-8{_}1332{_}He had taken sanctuary in the immense monastery adjoining the Golden Gate in the twelfth region of the city, founded for a thousand monks by the patrician Studius, in the year 463.
10095-8{_}1333{_}There he occupied himself with the concoction of poisons, the resource of fallen statesmen.
10095-8{_}1334{_}When a defeated minister of our own day is indisposed to accept his discomfiture, he applies himself to poison the public mind, inciting the lower orders against the higher, and blowing up every smouldering ember of sedition he can discover, trusting that the conflagration thus kindled, though it consume the edifice of the State, will not fail to roast his own egg.
10095-8{_}1335{_}Photinius's conceptions of mischief were less refined; he perfected his toxicological knowledge in the medical laboratory of the monastery, and sought eagerly for an opportunity of employing it; whether in an experiment upon the Emperor, or on his own successor, or on some other personage, circumstances must determine.
10095-8{_}1336{_}The sanctity of Studius's convent, and the strength of its monastic garrison, rendered it a safe refuge for disgraced courtiers, and in this thirtieth year of the Emperor Basil the Second (reckoning from his nominal accession) it harboured  a legion of ex-prime ministers, patriarchs, archbishops, chief secretaries, hypati, anthypati, silentiarii, protospatharii, and even spatharo-candidati.
10095-8{_}1337{_}And this small army was nothing to the host that, maimed or blinded or tonsured or all three, dragged out their lives in monasteries or in dungeons or on rocky islets; and these again were few in comparison with the spirits of the traitors or the betrayed who wailed nightly amid the planes and cypresses of the Aretae, or stalked through the palatial apartments of verdantique and porphyry.
10095-8{_}1338{_}But of those comparatively at liberty, but whose liberty was circumscribed by the hallowed precincts of Studius, every soul was plotting.
10095-8{_}1339{_}And never, perhaps, in the corrupt Byzantine Court, where true friendship had been unknown since Theodora quarrelled with Antonia, had so near an approach to it existed as in this asylum of villains.
10095-8{_}1340{_}A sort of freemasonry came to prevail in the sanctuary: every one longed to know how his neighbour's plot throve, and grudged not to buy the knowledge by disclosing a little corner of his own.
10095-8{_}1341{_}Thus rendered communicative, their colloquies would travel back into the past, and as the veterans of intrigue fought their battles over again, the most experienced would learn things that made them open their eyes with amazement.
10095-8{_}1342{_}"Ah!"
10095-8{_}1343{_}they would hear, "that is just where you were mistaken.
10095-8{_}1344{_}You had bought Eromenus, but so had I, and old Nicephorus had outbid us both."
10095-8{_}1345{_}"You deemed the dancer Anthusa a sure card, and knew not of her secret infirmity, of which I had been apprised by her waiting woman."
10095-8{_}1346{_}"Did you really know nothing of that sliding panel?
10095-8{_}1347{_}And were you ignorant  that whatever one says in the blue chamber is heard in the green?"
10095-8{_}1348{_}"Yes, I thought so too, and I spent a mint of money before finding out that the dog whose slaver that brazen impostor Panurgiades pretended to sell me was no more mad than he was."
10095-8{_}1349{_}After such rehearsals of future dialogues by the banks of Styx, the fallen statesmen were observed to appear exceedingly dejected, but the stimulus had become necessary to their existence.
10095-8{_}1350{_}None gossiped so freely or disclosed so much as Photinius and his predecessor Eustathius, whom he had himself displaced--probably because Eustathius, believing in nothing in heaven or earth but gold, and labouring under an absolute privation of that metal, was regarded even by himself as an extinct volcano.
10095-8{_}1351{_}"Well," observed he one day, when discoursing with Photinius is an unusually confidential mood, "I am free to say that for my own part I don't think over much of poison.
10095-8{_}1352{_}It has its advantages, to be sure, but to my mind the disadvantages are even more conspicuous."
10095-8{_}1353{_}"For example?"
10095-8{_}1354{_}inquired Photinius, who had the best reason for confiding in the efficacy of a drag administered with dexterity and discretion.
10095-8{_}1355{_}"Two people must be in the secret at least, if not three," replied Eustathius, "and cooks, as a rule, are a class of persons entirely unfit to be employed in affairs of State."
10095-8{_}1356{_}"The Court physician," suggested Photinius.
10095-8{_}1357{_}"Is only available," answered Eustathius, "in case his Majesty should send for him, which is most improbable.
10095-8{_}1358{_}If he ever did, poison, praised be the Lord!
10095-8{_}1359{_}would be totally unnecessary and entirely superfluous."
10095-8{_}1360{_}"My dear friend," said Photinius, venturing at this favourable moment on a question he had been dying to ask ever since he had been an inmate of the convent, "would you mind telling me in confidence, did you ever administer any potion of a deleterious nature to his Sacred Majesty?"
10095-8{_}1361{_}"Never!"
10095-8{_}1362{_}protested Eustathius, with fervour.
10095-8{_}1363{_}"I tried once, to be sure, but it was no use."
10095-8{_}1364{_}"What was the impediment?"
10095-8{_}1365{_}"The perverse opposition of the cupbearer.
10095-8{_}1366{_}It is idle attempting anything of the kind as long as she is about the Emperor."
10095-8{_}1367{_}"_She_!"
10095-8{_}1368{_}exclaimed Photinius.
10095-8{_}1369{_}"Don't you know _that_?"
10095-8{_}1370{_}responded Eustathius, with an air and manner that plainly said, "You don't know much."
10095-8{_}1371{_}Humbled and ashamed, Photinius nevertheless wisely stooped to avow his nescience, and flattering his rival on his superior penetration, led him to divulge the State secret that the handsome cupbearer Helladius was but the disguise of the lovely Helladia, the object of Basil's tenderest affection, and whose romantic attachment to his person had already frustrated more conspiracies than the aged plotter could reckon up.
10095-8{_}1372{_}This intelligence made Photinius for a season exceedingly thoughtful.
10095-8{_}1373{_}He had not deemed Basil of an amorous complexion.
10095-8{_}1374{_}At length he sent for his daughter, the beautiful and virtuous Euprepia, who from time to time visited him in the monastery.
10095-8{_}1375{_}"Daughter," he said, "it appears to me that the time has now arrived when thou mayest with propriety present a petition to the Emperor on behalf of thy unfortunate father.
10095-8{_}1376{_}Here is the  document.
10095-8{_}1377{_}It is, I flatter myself, composed with no ordinary address; nevertheless I will not conceal from thee that I place my hopes rather on thy beauty of person than on my beauty of style.
10095-8{_}1378{_}Shake down thy hair and dishevel it, so!--that is excellent.
10095-8{_}1379{_}Remember to tear thy robe some little in the poignancy of thy woe, and to lose a sandal.
10095-8{_}1380{_}Tears and sobs of course thou hast always at command, but let not the frenzy of thy grief render thee wholly inarticulate.
10095-8{_}1381{_}Here is a slight memorandum of what is most fitting for thee to say: thy old nurse's instructions will do the rest.
10095-8{_}1382{_}Light a candle for St. Sergius, and watch for a favourable opportunity."
10095-8{_}1383{_}Euprepia was upright, candid, and loyal; but the best of women has something of the actress in her nature; and her histrionic talent was stimulated by her filial affection.
10095-8{_}1384{_}Basil was for a moment fairly carried away by the consummate fact of her performance and the genuine feeling to her appeal; but he was himself again by the time he had finished perusing his late minister's long-winded and mendacious memorial.
10095-8{_}1385{_}"What manner of woman was thy mother?"
10095-8{_}1386{_}he inquired kindly  Euprepia was eloquent in praise of her deceased parent's perfections of mind and person.
10095-8{_}1387{_}"Then I can believe thee Photinius's daughter, which I might otherwise have doubted," returned Basil.
10095-8{_}1388{_}"As concerns him, I can only say, if he feels himself innocent, let him come out of sanctuary, and stand his trial.
10095-8{_}1389{_}But I will give thee a place at Court."
10095-8{_}1390{_}This was about all that Photinius hoped to obtain, and he joyfully consented to his daughter's  entering the Imperial court, exulting at having got in the thin end of the wedge.
10095-8{_}1391{_}She was attached to the person of the Emperor's sister-in-law, the "Slayer of the Bulgarians" himself being a most determined bachelor.
10095-8{_}1392{_}Time wore on.
10095-8{_}1393{_}Euprepia's opportunities of visiting her father were less frequent than formerly.
10095-8{_}1394{_}At last she came, looking thoroughly miserable, distracted, and forlorn.
10095-8{_}1395{_}"What ails thee, child?"
10095-8{_}1396{_}he inquired anxiously.
10095-8{_}1397{_}"Oh, father, in what a frightful position do I find myself!"
10095-8{_}1398{_}"Speak," he said, "and rely on my counsel."
10095-8{_}1399{_}"When I entered the Court," she proceeded, "I found at first but one human creature I could love or trust, and he--let me so call him--seemed to make up for the deficiencies of all the rest.
10095-8{_}1400{_}It was the cupbearer Helladius."
10095-8{_}1401{_}"I hope he is still thy friend," interrupted Photinius.
10095-8{_}1402{_}"The good graces of an Imperial cupbearer are always important, and I would have bought those of Helladius with a myriad of bezants."
10095-8{_}1403{_}"They were not to be thus obtained, father," said she.
10095-8{_}1404{_}"The purest disinterestedness, the noblest integrity, the most unselfish devotion, were the distinction of my friend.
10095-8{_}1405{_}And such beauty!
10095-8{_}1406{_}I cannot, I must not conceal that my heart was soon entirely his.
10095-8{_}1407{_}But--most strange it seemed to me then--it was long impossible for me to tell whether Helladius loved me or loved me not.
10095-8{_}1408{_}The most perfect sympathy existed between us: we seemed one heart and one soul: and yet, and yet, Helladius never gave the slightest indication of the sentiments which a young man might  be supposed to entertain for a young girl.
10095-8{_}1409{_}Vainly did I try every innocent wile that a modest maiden may permit herself: he was ever the friend, never the lover.
10095-8{_}1410{_}At length, after long pining between despairing fondness and wounded pride, I myself turned away, and listened to one who left me in no doubt of the sincerity of his passion."
10095-8{_}1411{_}"Who?"
10095-8{_}1412{_}"The Emperor!
10095-8{_}1413{_}And, to shorten the story of my shame, I became his mistress."
10095-8{_}1414{_}"The saints be praised!"
10095-8{_}1415{_}shouted Photinius.
10095-8{_}1416{_}"O my incomparable daughter!"
10095-8{_}1417{_}"Father!"
10095-8{_}1418{_}cried Euprepia, blushing and indignant.
10095-8{_}1419{_}"But let me hurry on with my wretched tale.
10095-8{_}1420{_}In proportion as the Emperor's affection became more marked, Helladius, hitherto so buoyant and serene, became a visible prey to despondency.
10095-8{_}1421{_}Some scornful beauty, I deemed, was inflicting on him the tortures he had previously inflicted upon me, and, cured of my unhappy attachment, and entirely devoted to my Imperial lover, I did all in my power to encourage him.
10095-8{_}1422{_}He received my comfort with gratitude, nor did it, as I had feared might happen, seem to excite the least lover-like feeling towards me on his own part."
10095-8{_}1423{_}"Euprepia," he said only two days ago, "never in this Court have I met one like thee.
10095-8{_}1424{_}Thou art the soul of honour and generosity.
10095-8{_}1425{_}I can safely trust thee with a secret which my bursting heart can no longer retain, but which I dread to breathe even to myself.
10095-8{_}1426{_}Know first I am not what I seem, I am a woman!"
10095-8{_}1427{_}And opening his vest--"  "We know all about that already," interrupted Photinius.

10095-8{_}1429{_}"If thou knowest this already, father," said the astonished Euprepia, "thou wilt spare me the pain of entering further into Helladia's affection for Basil.
10095-8{_}1430{_}Suffice that it was impassioned beyond description, and vied with whatever history or romance records.
10095-8{_}1431{_}In her male costume she had accompanied the conqueror of the Bulgarians in his campaigns, she had fought in his battles; a gigantic foe, in act to strike him from behind, had fallen by her arrow; she had warded the poison-cup from his lips, and the assassin's dagger from his heart; she had rejected enormous wealth offered as a bribe for treachery, and lived only for the Emperor.
10095-8{_}1432{_}'And now,' she cried, 'his love for me is cold, and he deserts me for another.
10095-8{_}1433{_}Who she is I cannot find, else on her it were, not on him, that my vengeance should alight.
10095-8{_}1434{_}Oh, Euprepia, I would tear her eyes from her head, were they beautiful as thine!
10095-8{_}1435{_}But vengeance I must have.
10095-8{_}1436{_}Basil must die.
10095-8{_}1437{_}On the third day he expires by my hand, poisoned by the cup which I alone am trusted to offer him at the Imperial banquet where thou wilt be present.
10095-8{_}1438{_}Thou shalt see his agonies and my triumph, and rejoice that thy friend has known how to avenge herself.'
10095-8{_}1439{_}"Thou seest now, father, in how frightful a difficulty I am placed.
10095-8{_}1440{_}All my entreaties and remonstrances have been in vain: at my threats Helladia merely laughs.
10095-8{_}1441{_}I love Basil with my whole heart.
10095-8{_}1442{_}Shall I look on and see him murdered?
10095-8{_}1443{_}Shall I, having first unwittingly done my friend the most grievous injury, proceed further to betray her, and doom her to a cruel death?
10095-8{_}1444{_}I might anticipate her fell purpose by slaying her, but for that I have neither strength  nor courage.
10095-8{_}1445{_}Many a time have I felt on the point of revealing everything to her, and offering myself as her victim, but for this also I lack fortitude.
10095-8{_}1446{_}I might convey a warning to Basil, but Helladia's vengeance is unsleeping, and nothing but her death or mine will screen him.
10095-8{_}1447{_}Oh, father, father!
10095-8{_}1448{_}what am I to do?"
10095-8{_}1449{_}"Nothing romantic or sentimental, I trust, dear child," replied Photinius.
10095-8{_}1450{_}"Torture me not, father.
10095-8{_}1451{_}I came to thee for counsel."
10095-8{_}1452{_}"And counsel shalt thou have, but it must be the issue of mature deliberation.
10095-8{_}1453{_}Thou mayest observe," continued he with the air of a good man contending with adversity, "how weak and miserable is man's estate even in the day of good fortune, how hard it is for purblind mortals to discern the right path, especially when two alluring routes are simultaneously presented for their decision!
10095-8{_}1454{_}The most obvious and natural course, the one I should have adopted without hesitation half-an-hour ago, would be simply to let Helladia alone.

10095-8{_}1471{_}Let me consider.

10095-8{_}1482{_}"Poor child," he said, "thy nerves are unstrung, and no wonder.
10095-8{_}1483{_}It is a terrible risk to run.
10095-8{_}1484{_}Even if thou saidest nothing, and Helladia under the torture accused thee of having been privy to her design, it might have a bad effect on the Emperor's mind.
10095-8{_}1485{_}If he put thee to the torture too--but no!
10095-8{_}1486{_}that's impossible.
10095-8{_}1487{_}I feel faint and giddy, dear child, and unable to decide a point of such importance.
10095-8{_}1488{_}Come to me at daybreak to-morrow."
10095-8{_}1489{_}But Euprepia did not reappear, and Photinius spent the day in an agony of expectation, fearing that she had compromised herself by some imprudence.
10095-8{_}1490{_}He gazed on the setting sun with uncontrollable impatience, knowing that it would shine on the Imperial banquet, where so much was to happen.
10095-8{_}1491{_}Basil was in fact at that very moment seating himself among a brilliant assemblage.
10095-8{_}1492{_}By his side stood a choir of musicians, among them Euprepia.
10095-8{_}1493{_}Soon the cup was called for, and Helladia, in her masculine dress, stepped forward, darting a glance of sinister triumph at her friend.
10095-8{_}1494{_}Silently, almost imperceptibly to the bulk of the company, Euprepia glided forward, and hissed rather than whispered in Helladia's ear, ere she could retire from the Emperor's side.
10095-8{_}1495{_}"Didst thou not say that if thou couldst discover her who had wronged thee, thou wouldst wreak thy vengeance on her, and molest Basil no further?"
10095-8{_}1496{_}"I did, and I meant it."
10095-8{_}1497{_}"See that thou keepest thy word.
10095-8{_}1498{_}I am she!"
10095-8{_}1499{_}And snatching the cup from the table, she quaffed  it to the last drop, and instantly expired in convulsions.
10095-8{_}1500{_}We pass over the dismay of the banqueters, the arrest and confession of Helladia, the general amazement at the revelation of her sex, the frantic grief of the Emperor.
10095-8{_}1501{_}Basil's sorrow was sincere and durable.
10095-8{_}1502{_}On an early occasion he thus addressed his courtiers:  "I cannot determine which of these two women loved me best: she who gave her life for me, or she who would have taken mine.
10095-8{_}1503{_}The first made the greater sacrifice; the second did most violence to her feelings.
10095-8{_}1504{_}What say ye?"
10095-8{_}1505{_}The courtiers hesitated, feeling themselves incompetent judges in problems of this nature.
10095-8{_}1506{_}At length the youngest exclaimed:  "O Emperor, how can we tell thee, unless we know what thou thinkest thyself?"
10095-8{_}1507{_}"What!"
10095-8{_}1508{_}exclaimed Basil, "an honest man in the Court of Byzantium!
10095-8{_}1509{_}Let his mouth be filled with gold immediately!"
10095-8{_}1510{_}This operation having been performed, and the precious metal distributed in fees among the proper officers, Basil thus addressed the object of his favour:  "Manuel, thy name shall henceforth be Chrysostomus, in memory of what has just taken place.
10095-8{_}1511{_}In further token of my approbation of thy honesty, I will confer upon thee the hand of the only other respectable person about the Court, namely, of Helladia.
10095-8{_}1512{_}Take her, my son, and raise up a race of heroes!
10095-8{_}1513{_}She shall be amply dowered out of what remains of the property of Photinius."
10095-8{_}1514{_}"Gennadius," whispered a cynical courtier to  his neighbour, "I hope thou admirest the magnanimity of our sovereign, who deems he is performing a most generous action in presenting Manuel with his cast-off mistress, who has tried to poison him, and with whom he has been at his wits' end what to do, and in dowering her at the expense of another."
10095-8{_}1515{_}The snarl was just; but it is just also to acknowledge that Basil, as a prince born in the purple, had not the least idea that he was laying himself open to any such criticism.
10095-8{_}1516{_}He actually did feel the manly glow of self-approbation which accompanies the performance of a good action: an emotion which no one else present, except Chrysostomus, was so much as able to conceive.
10095-8{_}1517{_}It is further to be remarked that the old courtier who sneered at Chrysostomus was devoured by envy of his good fortune, and would have given his right eye to have been in his place.
10095-8{_}1518{_}"Chrysostomus," pursued Basil, "we must now think of the hapless Photinius.
10095-8{_}1519{_}That unfortunate father is doubtless in an agony of grief which renders the forfeiture of the remains of his possessions indifferent to him.
10095-8{_}1520{_}Thou, his successor therein, mayest be regarded as in some sort his son-in-law.
10095-8{_}1521{_}Go, therefore, and comfort him, and report to me upon his condition."
10095-8{_}1522{_}Chrysostomus accordingly proceeded to the monastery, where he was informed that Photinius had retired with his spiritual adviser, and could on no account be disturbed.
10095-8{_}1523{_}"It is on my head to see the Emperor's orders obeyed," returned Chrysostomus, and forced the door.
10095-8{_}1524{_}The bereaved parent was busily engaged in sticking pins into a wax effigy of Basil, under  the direction of Panurgiades, already honourably mentioned in this history.
10095-8{_}1525{_}"Wretched old man!"
10095-8{_}1526{_}exclaimed Chrysostomus, "is this thy grief for thy daughter?"
10095-8{_}1527{_}"My grief is great," answered Photinius, "but my time is small.
10095-8{_}1528{_}If I turn not every moment to account, I shall never be prime minister again.
10095-8{_}1529{_}But all is over now.
10095-8{_}1530{_}Thou wilt denounce me, of course.
10095-8{_}1531{_}I will give thee a counsel.
10095-8{_}1532{_}Say that thou didst arrive just as we were about to place the effigy of Basil before a slow fire, and melt it into a caldron of bubbling poison."
10095-8{_}1533{_}"I shall report what I have seen," replied Chrysostomus, "neither more nor less.
10095-8{_}1534{_}But I think I can assure thee that none will suffer for this mummery except Panurgiades, and that he will at most be whipped."
10095-8{_}1535{_}"Chrysostomus," said Basil, on receiving the report, "lust of power, a fever in youth, is a leprosy in age.
10095-8{_}1536{_}The hoary statesman out of place would sell his daughter, his country, his soul, to regain it: yea, he would part with his skin and his senses, were it possible to hold office without them.
10095-8{_}1537{_}I commiserate Photinius, whose faculties are clearly on the decline; the day has been when he would not have wasted his time sticking pins into a waxen figure.
10095-8{_}1538{_}I will give him some shadow of authority to amuse his old days and keep him out of mischief.
10095-8{_}1539{_}The Abbot of Catangion is just dead.
10095-8{_}1540{_}Photinius shall succeed him."
10095-8{_}1541{_}So Photinius received the tonsure and the dignity, and made a very tolerable Abbot.
10095-8{_}1542{_}It is even recorded to his honour that he bestowed a handsome funeral on his old enemy Eustathius.
10095-8{_}1543{_}Helladia made Chrysostomus an excellent wife, a little over-prudish, some thought.
10095-8{_}1544{_}When, nearly two centuries afterwards, the Courts of Love came to be established in Provence, the question at issue between her and Euprepia was referred to those tribunals, which, finding the decision difficult, adjourned it for seven hundred years.
10095-8{_}1545{_}That period having now expired, it is submitted to the British public.
10095-8{_}1546{_}THE WISDOM OF THE INDIANS   Everybody knows that in the reign of the Emperor Elagabalus Rome was visited by an embassy from India; whose members, on their way from the East, had held that memorable interview with the illustrious (though heretical) Christian philosopher Bardesanes which enabled him to formulate his doctrine of Fate, borrowed from the Indian theory of Karma, and therefore, until lately, grievously misunderstood by his commentators.
10095-8{_}1547{_}It may not, however, be equally notorious that the ambassadors returned by sea as far as Berytus, and upon landing there were hospitably entertained by the sage Euphronius, the head of the philosophical faculty of that University.
10095-8{_}1548{_}Euphronius naturally inquired what circumstance in Rome had appeared to his visitors most worthy of remark.
10095-8{_}1549{_}"The extreme evil of the Emperor's Karma," said they.
10095-8{_}1550{_}Euphronius requested further explanation.
10095-8{_}1551{_}"Karma," explained their interpreter, "is that congeries of circumstances which has necessitated the birth of each individual, and of whose good or evil he is the incarnation.
10095-8{_}1552{_}Every act must needs be attended by consequences, and as these are usually of too far-reaching a character to be  exhausted in the life of the doer of the action, they cannot but engender another person by whom they are to be borne.
10095-8{_}1553{_}This truth is popularly expressed by the doctrine of transmigration, according to which individuals, as the character of their deeds may determine, are re-born as pigs or peacocks, beggars or princes.
10095-8{_}1554{_}But this is a loose and unscientific way of speaking, for in fact it is not the individual that is re-born, but the character; which, even as the silkworm clothes itself with silk and the caddis-worm with mud and small shingle, creates for itself a new personality, congruous with its own nature.
10095-8{_}1555{_}We are therefore led to reflect what a prodigious multitude of sins some one must have committed ere the Roman world could be afflicted with such an Emperor as Elagabalus."
10095-8{_}1556{_}"What have ye found so exceedingly reprehensible in the Emperor's conduct?"
10095-8{_}1557{_}demanded Euphronius.
10095-8{_}1558{_}"To speak only," said the Indians, "of such of his doings as may fitly be recited to modest ears, we find him declaring war against Nature, and delighting in nothing that is not the contrary of what Heaven meant it to be.
10095-8{_}1559{_}We see him bathing in perfumes, sailing ships in wine, feeding horses on grapes and lions on parrots, peppering fish with pearls, wearing gems on the soles of his feet, strewing his floor with gold-dust, paving the public streets with precious marbles, driving teams of stags, scorning to eat fish by the seaside, deploring his lot that he has never yet been able to dine on a phoenix.
10095-8{_}1560{_}Enormous must have been the folly and wickedness which has incarnated itself in such a sovereign, and should his reign be  prolonged, discouraging is the prospect for the morals of the next generation.
10095-8{_}1561{_}"According to you, then," said Euphronius, "the fates of men are not spun for them by Clotho, Lachesis, and Atropos, but by their predecessors?"
10095-8{_}1562{_}"So it is," said they, "always remembering that man can rid himself of his Karma by philosophic meditation, combined with religious austerities, and that if all walked in this path, existence with all its evils would come to an end.
10095-8{_}1563{_}Insomuch that the most bloodthirsty conqueror that ever devastated the earth hath not destroyed one thousandth part as many existences as the Lord Buddha."
10095-8{_}1564{_}"These are abstruse matters," said Euphronius, "and I lament that your stay in Berytus will not be long enough to instruct me adequately therein."
10095-8{_}1565{_}"Accompany us to India," said they, "and thou shalt receive instruction at the fountain head."
10095-8{_}1566{_}"I am old and feeble," apologised Euphronius, "and adjusted by long habit to my present environment.
10095-8{_}1567{_}Nevertheless I will propound the enterprise to my pupils, only somewhat repressing their ardour, lest the volunteers should be inconveniently numerous."
10095-8{_}1568{_}When, however, the proposition was made not a soul responded; though Euphronius reproached his disciples severely, and desired them to compare their want of spirit with his own thirst for knowledge, which, when he was a young man, had taken him as far as Alexandria to hear a celebrated rhetorician.
10095-8{_}1569{_}In the evening, however, two disciples came to him together, and professed their readiness to undertake the expedition, if promised  a reward commensurate with its danger and difficulty.
10095-8{_}1570{_}"Ye would learn the secret of my celebrated dilemma," said he, "which no sophist can elude?
10095-8{_}1571{_}'Tis much; 'tis immoderate; 'tis enormous; nevertheless, bring the wisdom of India to Berytus, and the knowledge of the stratagem shall be yours."
10095-8{_}1572{_}"No, Master," they said, "it is not thy dilemma of which we are enamoured.
10095-8{_}1573{_}It is thy daughter."
10095-8{_}1574{_}A vehement altercation ensued, but at length the old philosopher, who at the bottom of his heart was much readier to part with his daughter than his dilemma, was induced to promise her to whichever of the pupils should bring home the most satisfactory exposition of Indian metaphysics: provided always that during their absence he should not have been compelled to bestow her hand as the price of a quibble even more subtle than his own: but this he believed to be impossible.
10095-8{_}1575{_}Mnesitheus and Rufus accordingly travelled with the embassy to India, and arrived in safety at the metropolis of Palimbothra.
10095-8{_}1576{_}They had wisely devoted themselves meanwhile to learning the language, and were now able to converse with some fluency.
10095-8{_}1577{_}On reaching their destination they were placed under the superintendence of competent instructors, who were commissioned to initiate them into the canon of Buddhist scriptures, comprising, to mention only a few of the principal, the Lalitavistara, the Dhammapada, the Kuddhapatha, the Palinokkha, the Uragavagga, the Kulavagga, the Mahavagga, the Atthakavagga, and the Upasampadakammavaca.
10095-8{_}1578{_}These works, composed in dead languages, and written in strange and unknown characters, were further provided with commentaries more voluminous and inexplicable than the text.
10095-8{_}1579{_}"Heavens," exclaimed Mnesitheus and Rufus, "can the life of a man suffice to study all this?"
10095-8{_}1580{_}"Assuredly not," replied the Indians.
10095-8{_}1581{_}"The diligent student will resume his investigations in a subsequent stage of existence, and, if endowed with eminent faculties, may hope to attain the end he proposes to himself at the fifteenth transmigration."
10095-8{_}1582{_}"The end we propose to ourselves," said the Greeks, "is to marry our master's daughter.
10095-8{_}1583{_}Will the fair Euphronia also have undergone fifteen transmigrations, and will her charms have continued unimpaired?"
10095-8{_}1584{_}"It is difficult to pronounce," said they, "for should the maiden, through the exercise of virtue, have merited to be born as a white elephant, her transmigrations must in the order of nature be but few; whereas should she have unfortunately become and remained a rat, a frog, or other shortlived animal, they cannot but be exceedingly numerous."
10095-8{_}1585{_}"The prospect of wedding a frog at the end of fifteen transmigrations," said the youths, "doth not in any respect commend itself to us.
10095-8{_}1586{_}Are there no means by which the course of study may be accelerated?"
10095-8{_}1587{_}"Undoubtedly," said the Indians, "by the practice of religious austerities."
10095-8{_}1588{_}"Of what nature are these?"
10095-8{_}1589{_}inquired the young men.
10095-8{_}1590{_}"The intrepid disciple," said the sages, "may chain himself to a tree, and gaze upon the sun until he is deprived of the faculty of vision.
10095-8{_}1591{_}He may drive an iron bar through his cheeks and tongue, thus preventing all misuse of the gift of speech.
10095-8{_}1592{_}It is open to him to bury himself in the earth up to his waist, relying for his maintenance on the alms of pious donors.
10095-8{_}1593{_}He may recline upon a couch studded with spikes, until from the induration of his skin he shall have merited the title of a rhinoceros among sages.
10095-8{_}1594{_}As, however, these latter practices interfere with locomotion, and thus prevent his close attendance on his spiritual guide, it is rather recommended to him to elevate his arms above his head, and retain them in that position until, by the withering of the sinews, it is impossible for him to bring them down again."
10095-8{_}1595{_}"In that case," cried Rufus, "farewell philosophy!
10095-8{_}1596{_}farewell Euphronia!"
10095-8{_}1597{_}There is reason to believe that Mnesitheus would have made exactly the same observation if Rufus had not been beforehand with him.
10095-8{_}1598{_}The spirit of contradiction and the affectation of superiority, however, led him to reproach his rival with pusillanimity, and he went so far that at length he found himself committed to undergo the ordeal: merely stipulating that, in consideration of his being a foreigner, he should be permitted to elevate the right arm only.
10095-8{_}1599{_}The king of the country most graciously came to his assistance by causing him to be fastened to a tree, with his uplifted arm secured by iron bands above his head, a fan being put in his other hand to protect him against the molestations of gnats  and mosquitoes.
10095-8{_}1600{_}By this means, and with the assistance of the monks who continually recited and expounded the Buddhist scriptures in his ears, some time even before his arm had stiffened for ever, the doctrine of the misery of existence had become perfectly clear to him.
10095-8{_}1601{_}Released from his captivity, he hastened back to Europe to claim the guerdon of his sufferings.
10095-8{_}1602{_}History is silent respecting his adventures until his arrival at Berytus, where the strange wild-looking man with the uplifted arm found himself the centre of a turbulent and mischievous rabble.
10095-8{_}1603{_}As he seemed about to suffer severe ill-usage, a personage of dignified and portly appearance hastened up, and with his staff showered blows to right and left upon the rioters.
10095-8{_}1604{_}"Scoundrels," he exclaimed, "finely have ye profited by my precepts, thus to misuse an innocent stranger!
10095-8{_}1605{_}But I will no longer dwell among such barbarians.
10095-8{_}1606{_}I will remove my school to Tarsus!"
10095-8{_}1607{_}The mob dispersed.
10095-8{_}1608{_}The victim and his deliverer stood face to face.
10095-8{_}1609{_}"Mnesitheus!"
10095-8{_}1610{_}"Rufus!"
10095-8{_}1611{_}"Call me Rufinianus," corrected the latter; "for such is the appellation which I have felt it due to myself to assume, since the enhancement of my dignity by becoming Euphronius's successor and son-in-law."
10095-8{_}1612{_}"Son-in-law!
10095-8{_}1613{_}Am I to lose the reward of my incredible sufferings?"
10095-8{_}1614{_}"Thou forgettest," said Rufinianus, "that Euphronia's hand was not promised as the reward of any austerities, but as the meed of the most  intelligent, that is, the most acceptable, account of the Indian philosophy, which in the opinion of the late eminent Euphronius, has been delivered by me.
10095-8{_}1615{_}But come to my chamber, and let me minister to thy necessities."
10095-8{_}1616{_}These having been duly attended to, Rufinianus demanded Mnesitheus's history, and then proceeded to narrate his own.
10095-8{_}1617{_}"On my journey homeward," said he, "I reflected seriously on the probable purpose of our master in sending us forth, and saw reason to suspect that I had hitherto misapprehended it.
10095-8{_}1618{_}For I could not remember that he had ever admitted that he could have anything to learn from other philosophers, or that he had ever exhibited the least interest in philosophic dogmas, excepting his own.
10095-8{_}1619{_}The system of the Indians, I thought, must be either inferior to that of Euphronius, or superior.
10095-8{_}1620{_}If the former, he will not want it: if the latter, he will want it much less.
10095-8{_}1621{_}I therefore concluded that our mission was partly a concession to public opinion, partly to enable him to say that his name was known, and his teaching proclaimed on the very banks of the Ganges.
10095-8{_}1622{_}I formed my plan accordingly, and disregarding certain indications that I was neither expected nor wanted, presented myself before Euphronius with a gladsome countenance, slightly overcast by sorrow on account of thee, whom I affirmed to have been devoured by a tiger.
10095-8{_}1623{_}"'Well,' said Euphronius in a disdainful tone, 'and what about this vaunted wisdom of the Indians?'
10095-8{_}1624{_}"'The wisdom of the Indians,' I replied, 'is entirely borrowed from Pythagoras.'
10095-8{_}1625{_}"'Did I not tell you so?
10095-8{_}1626{_}'Euphronius appealed to his disciples.
10095-8{_}1627{_}"'Invariably,' they replied.
10095-8{_}1628{_}"'As if a barbarian could teach a Greek!'
10095-8{_}1629{_}said he.
10095-8{_}1630{_}"'It is much if he is able to learn from one,' said they.
10095-8{_}1631{_}"'Pythagoras, then,' said Euphronius addressing me,' did not resort to India to be instructed by the Gymnosophists?'
10095-8{_}1632{_}"'On the contrary,' I answered, 'he went there to teach them, and the little knowledge of divine matters they possess is entirely derived from him.
10095-8{_}1633{_}His mission is recorded in a barbarous poem called the Ramayana, wherein he is figuratively represented as allying himself with monkeys.
10095-8{_}1634{_}He is worshipped all over the country under the appellations of Siva, Kamadeva, Kali, Gautama Buddha, and others too numerous to mention.'
10095-8{_}1635{_}"When I further proceeded to explain that a temple had been erected to Euphronius himself on the banks of the Ganges, and that a festival, called Durga Popja, or the Feast of Reason, had been instituted in his honour, his good humour knew no bounds, and he granted me his daughter's hand without difficulty.
10095-8{_}1636{_}He died a few years ago, bequeathing me his celebrated dilemma, and I am now head of his school and founder of the Rufinianian philosophy.
10095-8{_}1637{_}I am also the author of some admired works, especially a life of Pythagoras, and a manual of Indian philosophy and religion.
10095-8{_}1638{_}I hope for thy own sake thou wilt forbear to contradict me: for no one will believe thee.
10095-8{_}1639{_}I trust also that thou wilt speedily overcome thy disappointment with respect to Euphronia.
10095-8{_}1640{_}I do most  honestly and truthfully assure thee that for a one-armed man like thee to marry her would be most inexpedient, inasmuch as the defence of one's beard from her, when she is in a state of excitement, requires the full use of both hands, and of the feet also.
10095-8{_}1641{_}But come with me to her chamber, and I will present thee to her.
10095-8{_}1642{_}She is always taunting me with my inferiority to thee in personal attractions, and I promise myself much innocent amusement from her discomfiture when she finds thee as gaunt as a wolf and as black as a cinder.
10095-8{_}1643{_}Only, as I have represented thee to have been devoured by a tiger, thou wilt kindly say that I saved thy life, but concealed the circumstance out of modesty."
10095-8{_}1644{_}"I have learned in the Indian schools," said Mnesitheus, "not to lie for the benefit of others.
10095-8{_}1645{_}I will not see Euphronia; I would not disturb her ideal of me, nor mine of her.
10095-8{_}1646{_}Farewell.
10095-8{_}1647{_}May the Rufinianian sect flourish!
10095-8{_}1648{_}and may thy works on Pythagoras and India instruct posterity to the tenth generation!
10095-8{_}1649{_}I return to Palimbothra, where I am held in honour on the self-same account that here renders me ridiculous.
10095-8{_}1650{_}It shall be my study to enlighten the natives respecting their obligations to Pythagoras, whose name I did not happen to hear while I abode among them."
10095-8{_}1651{_}THE DUMB ORACLE     Many the Bacchi that brandish the rod:   Few that be filled with the fire of the God.
10095-8{_}1652{_}I   In the days of King Attalus, before oracles had lost their credit, one of peculiar reputation, inspired, as was believed, by Apollo, existed in the city of Dorylseum, in Phrygia.
10095-8{_}1653{_}Contrary to usage, its revelations were imparted through the medium of a male priest.
10095-8{_}1654{_}It was rarely left unthronged by devout questioners, whose inquiries were resolved in writing, agreeably to the method delivered by the pious Lucian, in his work "Concerning False Prophecy."
10095-8{_}1655{_}[*] Sometimes, on extraordinary occasions, a voice, evidently that of the deity, was heard declaring the response from the innermost recesses of the shrine.
10095-8{_}1656{_}The treasure house of the sanctuary was stored with tripods and goblets, in general wrought from the precious metals; its coffers were loaded with coins and ingots; the sacrifices of wealthy suppliants and the copious offerings in kind of the country people provided superabundantly for the daily maintenance of the temple servitors; while a rich endowment in land maintained the dignity of its guardians, and of the officiating priest.
10095-8{_}1657{_}The  latter reverend personage was no less eminent for prudence than for piety; on which account the Gods had rewarded him with extreme obesity.
10095-8{_}1658{_}At length he died, whether of excess in meat or in drink is not agreed among historians.
10095-8{_}1659{_}[Footnote: _Pseudomantis_, cap.
10095-8{_}1660{_}19-21.]
10095-8{_}1661{_}The guardians of the temple met to choose a successor, and, naturally desirous that the sanctity of the oracle should suffer no abatement, elected a young priest of goodly presence and ascetic life; the humblest, purest, most fervent, and most ingenuous of the sons of men.
10095-8{_}1662{_}So rare a choice might well be expected to be accompanied by some extraordinary manifestation, and, in fact, a prodigy took place which filled the sacred authorities with dismay.
10095-8{_}1663{_}The responses of the oracle ceased suddenly and altogether.
10095-8{_}1664{_}No revelation was vouchsafed to the pontiff in his slumbers; no access of prophetic fury constrained him to disclose the secrets of the future; no voice rang from the shrine; and the unanswered epistles of the suppliants lay a hopeless encumbrance on the great altar.
10095-8{_}1665{_}As a natural consequence they speedily ceased to arrive; the influx of offerings into the treasury terminated along with them; the temple-courts were bare of worshippers; and the only victims whose blood smoked within them were those slain by the priest himself, in the hope of appeasing the displeasure of Apollo.
10095-8{_}1666{_}The modest hierophant took all the blame upon his own shoulders; he did not doubt that he had excited the Deity's wrath by some mysterious but heinous pollution; and was confirmed in this opinion by the unanimous verdict of all whom he approached.
10095-8{_}1667{_}One day as he sat sadly in the temple, absorbed  in painful meditation, and pondering how he might best relieve himself of his sacred functions, he was startled by the now unwonted sound of a footstep, and, looking up, espied an ancient woman.
10095-8{_}1668{_}Her appearance was rather venerable than prepossessing.
10095-8{_}1669{_}He recognised her as one of the inferior ministers of the temple.
10095-8{_}1670{_}"Reverend mother," he addressed her, "doubtless thou comest to mingle with mine thy supplications to the Deity, that it may please him to indicate the cause, and the remedy of his wrath."
10095-8{_}1671{_}"No, son," returned the venerable personage, "I propose to occasion no such needless trouble to Apollo, or any other Divinity.
10095-8{_}1672{_}I hold within mine own hand the power of reviving the splendour of this forsaken sanctuary, and for such consideration as thou wilt thyself pronounce equitable, I am minded to impart the same unto thee."
10095-8{_}1673{_}And as the astonished priest made no answer, she continued:  "My price is one hundred pieces of gold."
10095-8{_}1674{_}"Wretch!"
10095-8{_}1675{_}exclaimed the priest indignantly, "thy mercenary demand alone proves the vanity of thy pretence of being initiated into the secrets of the Gods.
10095-8{_}1676{_}Depart my presence this moment!"
10095-8{_}1677{_}The old woman retired without a syllable of remonstrance, and the incident soon passed from the mind of the afflicted priest.
10095-8{_}1678{_}But on the following day, at the same hour, the aged woman again stood before him, and said:  "My price is _two_ hundred pieces of gold."
10095-8{_}1679{_}Again she was commanded to depart, and again obeyed without a murmur.
10095-8{_}1680{_}But the adventure now occasioned the priest much serious reflection.
10095-8{_}1681{_}To his excited fancy, the patient persistency of the crone began to assume something  of a supernatural character.
10095-8{_}1682{_}He considered that the ways of the Gods are not as our ways, and that it is rather the rule than the exception with them to accomplish their designs in the most circuitous manner, and by the most unlikely instruments.
10095-8{_}1683{_}He also reflected upon the history of the Sibyl and her books, and shuddered to think that unseasonable obstinacy might in the end cost the temple the whole of its revenues.
10095-8{_}1684{_}The result of his cogitations was a resolution, if the old woman should present herself on the following day, to receive her in a different manner.
10095-8{_}1685{_}Punctual to the hour she made her appearance, and croaked out, "My price is _three_ hundred pieces of gold."
10095-8{_}1686{_}"Venerable ambassadress of Heaven," said the priest, "thy boon is granted thee.
10095-8{_}1687{_}Relieve the anguish of my bosom as speedily as thou mayest."
10095-8{_}1688{_}The old woman's reply was brief and expressive.
10095-8{_}1689{_}It consisted in extending her open and hollow palm, into which the priest counted the three hundred pieces of gold with as much expedition as was compatible with the frequent interruptions necessitated by the crone's depositing each successive handful in a leather pouch; and the scrutiny, divided between jealousy and affection, which she bestowed on each individual coin.
10095-8{_}1690{_}"And now," said the priest, when the operation was at length completed, "fulfil thy share of the compact."
10095-8{_}1691{_}"The cause of the oracle's silence," returned the old woman, "is the unworthiness of the minister."
10095-8{_}1692{_}"Alas!
10095-8{_}1693{_}'tis even as I feared," sighed the priest.
10095-8{_}1694{_}"Declare now, wherein consists my sin?"
10095-8{_}1695{_}"It consists in this," replied the old woman, "that the beard of thy understanding is not yet grown; and that the egg-shell of thy inexperience is still sticking to the head of thy simplicity; and that thy brains bear no adequate proportion to the skull enveloping them; and in fine, lest I seem to speak overmuch in parables, or to employ a superfluity of epithets, that thou art an egregious nincompoop."
10095-8{_}1696{_}And as the amazed priest preserved silence, she pursued:  "Can aught be more shameful in a religious man than ignorance of the very nature of religion?
10095-8{_}1697{_}Not to know that the term, being rendered into the language of truth, doth therein signify deception practised by the few wise upon the many foolish, for the benefit of both, but more particularly the former?
10095-8{_}1698{_}O silly as the crowds who hitherto have brought their folly here, but now carry it elsewhere to the profit of wiser men than thou!
10095-8{_}1699{_}O fool!
10095-8{_}1700{_}to deem that oracles were rendered by Apollo!
10095-8{_}1701{_}How should this be, seeing that there is no such person?
10095-8{_}1702{_}Needs there, peradventure, any greater miracle for the decipherment of these epistles than a hot needle?
10095-8{_}1703{_}[*] As for the supernatural voice, it doth in truth proceed from a respectable, and in some sense a sacred personage, being mine own when I am concealed within a certain recess prepared for me by thy lamented predecessor, whose mistress I was in youth, and whose coadjutor I have been in age.
10095-8{_}1704{_}I am now ready to minister to thee in the latter capacity.
10095-8{_}1705{_}Be ruled by me; exchange thy abject superstition for common sense; thy childish simplicity for  discreet policy; thy unbecoming spareness for a majestic portliness; thy present ridiculous and uncomfortable situation for the repute of sanctity, and the veneration of men.
10095-8{_}1706{_}Thou wilt own that this is cheap at three hundred pieces."
10095-8{_}1707{_}[Footnote: Lucian.]
10095-8{_}1708{_}The young priest had hearkened to the crone's discourse with an expression of the most exquisite distress.
10095-8{_}1709{_}When she had finished, he arose, and disregarding his repulsive companion's efforts to detain him, departed hastily from the temple.
10095-8{_}1710{_}II   It was the young priest's purpose, as soon as he became capable of forming one, to place the greatest possible distance between himself and the city of Dorylæum.
10095-8{_}1711{_}The love of roaming insensibly grew upon him, and ere long his active limbs had borne him over a considerable portion of Asia.
10095-8{_}1712{_}His simple wants were easily supplied by the wild productions of the country, supplemented when needful by the proceeds of light manual labour.
10095-8{_}1713{_}By degrees the self-contempt which had originally stung him to desperation took the form of an ironical compassion for the folly of mankind, and the restlessness which had at first impelled him to seek relief in a change of scene gave place to a spirit of curiosity and observation.
10095-8{_}1714{_}He learned to mix freely with all orders of men, save one, and rejoiced to find the narrow mysticism which he had imbibed from his previous education gradually yielding to contact with the great world.
10095-8{_}1715{_}From one class of men, indeed, he learned nothing--the  priests, whose society he eschewed with scrupulous vigilance, nor did he ever enter the temples of the Gods.
10095-8{_}1716{_}Diviners, augurs, all that made any pretension whatever to a supernatural character, he held in utter abhorrence, and his ultimate return in the direction of his native country is attributed to his inability to persevere further in the path he was following without danger of encountering Chaldean soothsayers, or Persian magi, or Indian gymnosophists.
10095-8{_}1717{_}He cherished, however, no intention of returning to Phrygia, and was still at a considerable distance from that region, when one night, as he was sitting in the inn of a small country town, his ear caught a phrase which arrested his attention.
10095-8{_}1718{_}"As true as the oracle of Dorylæum."
10095-8{_}1719{_}The speaker was a countryman, who appeared to have been asseverating something regarded by the rest of the company as greatly in need of confirmation.
10095-8{_}1720{_}The sudden start and stifled cry of the ex-priest drew all eyes to him, and he felt constrained to ask, with the most indifferent air he could assume:  "Is the oracle of Dorylæum, then, so exceedingly renowned for veracity?"
10095-8{_}1721{_}"Whence comest thou to be ignorant of that?"
10095-8{_}1722{_}demanded the countryman, with some disdain.
10095-8{_}1723{_}"Hast thou never heard of the priest Eubulides?"
10095-8{_}1724{_}"Eubulides!"
10095-8{_}1725{_}exclaimed the young traveller, "that is my own name!"
10095-8{_}1726{_}"Thou mayest well rejoice, then," observed another of the guests, "to bear the name of one so holy and pure, and so eminently favoured by the happy Gods.
10095-8{_}1727{_}So handsome and dignified, moreover, as I may well assert who have often  beheld him discharging his sacred functions.
10095-8{_}1728{_}And truly, now that I scan thee more closely, the resemblance is marvellous.
10095-8{_}1729{_}Only that thy namesake bears with him a certain air of divinity, not equally conspicuous in thee."
10095-8{_}1730{_}"Divinity!"
10095-8{_}1731{_}exclaimed another.
10095-8{_}1732{_}"Aye, if Phoebus himself ministered at his own shrine, he could wear no more majestic semblance than Eubulides."
10095-8{_}1733{_}"Or predict the future more accurately," added a priest.
10095-8{_}1734{_}"Or deliver his oracles in more exquisite verse," subjoined a poet.
10095-8{_}1735{_}"Yet is it not marvellous," remarked another speaker, "that for some considerable time after his installation the good Eubulides was unable to deliver a single oracle?"
10095-8{_}1736{_}"Aye, and that the first he rendered should have foretold the death of an aged woman, one of the ministers of the temple."
10095-8{_}1737{_}"Ha!"
10095-8{_}1738{_}exclaimed Eubulides, "how was that?"
10095-8{_}1739{_}"He prognosticated her decease on the following day, which accordingly came to pass, from her being choked with a piece of gold, not lawfully appertaining to herself, which she was endeavouring to conceal under the root of her tongue."
10095-8{_}1740{_}"The Gods be praised for that!"
10095-8{_}1741{_}ejaculated Eubulides, under his breath.
10095-8{_}1742{_}"Pshaw!
10095-8{_}1743{_}as if there were Gods!
10095-8{_}1744{_}If they existed, would they tolerate this vile mockery?
10095-8{_}1745{_}To keep up the juggle--well, I know it must be so; but to purloin my name!
10095-8{_}1746{_}to counterfeit my person!
10095-8{_}1747{_}By all the Gods that are not, I will expose the cheat, or perish in the endeavour."
10095-8{_}1748{_}He arose early on the following morning and took his way towards the city of Dorylæum.
10095-8{_}1749{_}The further he progressed in this direction, the louder became the bruit of the oracle of Apollo, and the more emphatic the testimonies to the piety, prophetic endowments, and personal attractions of the priest Eubulides; his own resemblance to whom was the theme of continual remark.
10095-8{_}1750{_}On approaching the city, he found the roads swarming with throngs hastening to the temple, about to take part in a great religious ceremony to be held therein.
10095-8{_}1751{_}The seriousness of worship blended delightfully with the glee of the festival, and Eubulides, who at first regarded the gathering with bitter scorn, found his moroseness insensibly yielding to the poetic charm of the scene.
10095-8{_}1752{_}He could not but acknowledge that the imposture he panted to expose was at least the source of much innocent happiness, and almost wished that the importance of religion, considered as an engine of policy, had been offered to his contemplation from this point of view, instead of the sordid and revolting aspect in which it had been exhibited by the old woman.
10095-8{_}1753{_}In this ambiguous frame of mind he entered the temple.
10095-8{_}1754{_}Before the high altar stood the officiating priest, a young man, the image, yet not the image, of himself.
10095-8{_}1755{_}Lineament for lineament, the resemblance was exact, but over the stranger's whole figure was diffused an air of majesty, of absolute serenity and infinite superiority, which excluded every idea of deceit, and so awed the young priest that his purpose of rushing forward to denounce the impostor and drag him from the shrine was immediately and involuntarily relinquished.
10095-8{_}1756{_}As he stood confounded and irresolute, the melodious voice of the hierophant rang through the temple:  "Let the priest Eubulides stand forth."
10095-8{_}1757{_}This summons naturally caused the greatest astonishment in every one but Eubulides, who emerged as swiftly as he could from the swaying and murmuring crowd, and confronted his namesake at the altar.
10095-8{_}1758{_}A cry of amazement broke from the multitude as they beheld the pair, whose main distinction in the eyes of most was their garb.
10095-8{_}1759{_}But, as they gazed, the form of the officiating priest assumed colossal proportions; a circle of beams, dimming sunlight, broke forth around his head; hyacinthine locks clustered on his shoulders, his eyes sparkled with supernatural radiance; a quiver depended at his back; an unstrung bow occupied his hand; the majesty and benignity of his presence alike seemed augmented tenfold.
10095-8{_}1760{_}Eubulides and the crowd sank simultaneously on their knees, for all recognised Apollo.
10095-8{_}1761{_}All was silence for a space.
10095-8{_}1762{_}It was at length broken by Phoebus.
10095-8{_}1763{_}"Well, Eubulides," inquired he, with the bland raillery of an Immortal, "has it at length occurred to thee that I may have been long enough away from Parnassus, filling thy place here while thou hast been disporting thyself amid heretics and barbarians?"
10095-8{_}1764{_}The abashed Eubulides made no response.
10095-8{_}1765{_}The Deity continued:  "Deem not that thou hast in aught excited the displeasure of the Gods.
10095-8{_}1766{_}In deserting their altars for Truth's sake, thou didst render them the most acceptable of sacrifices, the only one, it  may be, by which they set much store.
10095-8{_}1767{_}But, Eubulides, take heed how thou again sufferest the unworthiness of men to overcome the instincts of thine own nature.
10095-8{_}1768{_}Thy holiest sentiments should not have been at the mercy of a knave.
10095-8{_}1769{_}If the oracle of Dorylæum was an imposture, hadst thou no oracle in thine own bosom?
10095-8{_}1770{_}If the voice of Religion was no longer breathed from the tripod, were the winds and waters silent, or had aught quenched the everlasting stars?
10095-8{_}1771{_}If there was no power to impose its mandates from without, couldst thou be unconscious of a power within?
10095-8{_}1772{_}If thou hadst nothing to reveal unto men, mightest thou not have found somewhat to propound unto them?
10095-8{_}1773{_}Know this, that thou hast never experienced a more truly religious emotion than that which led thee to form the design of overthrowing this my temple, the abode, as thou didst deem it, of fraud and superstition."
10095-8{_}1774{_}"But now, Phoebus," Eubulides ventured to reply, "shall I not return to the shrine purified by thy presence, and again officiate as thy unworthy minister?"
10095-8{_}1775{_}"No, Eubulides," returned Phoebus, with a smile; "silver is good, but not for ploughshares.
10095-8{_}1776{_}Thy strange experience, thy long wanderings, thy lonely meditations, and varied intercourse with men, have spoiled thee for a priest, while, as I would fain hope, qualifying thee for a sage.
10095-8{_}1777{_}Some worthy person may easily be found to preside over this temple; and by the aid of such inspiration as I may from time to time see meet to vouchsafe him, administer its affairs indifferently well.
10095-8{_}1778{_}Do thou, Eubulides, consecrate thy powers to a more august service than Apollo's, to one  that shall endure when Delphi and Delos know _his_ no more."
10095-8{_}1779{_}"To whose service, Phoebus?"
10095-8{_}1780{_}inquired Eubulides.
10095-8{_}1781{_}"To the service of Humanity, my son," responded Apollo.
10095-8{_}1782{_}DUKE VIRGIL    I   The citizens of Mantua were weary of revolutions.
10095-8{_}1783{_}They had acknowledged the suzerainty of the Emperor Frederick and shaken it off.
10095-8{_}1784{_}They had had a Podestà of their own and had shaken him off.
10095-8{_}1785{_}They had expelled a Papal Legate, incurring excommunication thereby.
10095-8{_}1786{_}They had tried dictators, consuls, prætors, councils of ten, and other numbers odd and even, and ere the middle of the thirteenth century were luxuriating in the enjoyment of perfect anarchy.
10095-8{_}1787{_}An assembly met daily in quest of a remedy, but its members were forbidden to propose anything old, and were unable to invent anything new.
10095-8{_}1788{_}"Why not consult Manto, the alchemist's daughter, our prophetess, our Sibyl?"
10095-8{_}1789{_}the young Benedetto asked at last.
10095-8{_}1790{_}"Why not?"
10095-8{_}1791{_}repeated Eustachio, an elderly man.
10095-8{_}1792{_}"Why not, indeed?"
10095-8{_}1793{_}interrogated Leonardo, a man of mature years.
10095-8{_}1794{_}All the speakers were noble.
10095-8{_}1795{_}Benedetto was Manto's lover; Eustachio her father's friend; Leonardo his creditor.
10095-8{_}1796{_}Their advice prevailed, and the three were chosen as a deputation to wait on the prophetess.
10095-8{_}1797{_}Before proceeding formally  on their embassy the three envoys managed to obtain private interviews, the two elder with Manto's father, the youth with Manto herself.
10095-8{_}1798{_}The creditor promised that if he became Duke by the alchemist's influence with his daughter he would forgive the debt; the friend went further, and vowed that he would pay it.
10095-8{_}1799{_}The old man promised his good word to both, but when he went to confer with his daughter he found her closeted with Benedetto, and returned without disburdening himself of his errand.
10095-8{_}1800{_}The youth had just risen from his knees, pleading with her, and drawing glowing pictures of their felicity when he should be Duke and she Duchess.
10095-8{_}1801{_}She answered, "Benedetto, in all Mantua there is not one man fit to rule another.
10095-8{_}1802{_}To name any living person would be to set a tyrant over my native city.
10095-8{_}1803{_}I will repair to the shades and seek a ruler among the dead."
10095-8{_}1804{_}"And why should not Mantua have a tyrant?"
10095-8{_}1805{_}demanded Benedetto.
10095-8{_}1806{_}"The freedom of the mechanic is the bondage of the noble, who values no liberty save that of making the base-born do his bidding.
10095-8{_}1807{_}'Tis hell to a man of spirit to be contradicted by his tailor.
10095-8{_}1808{_}If I could see my heart's desire on the knaves, little would I reck submitting to the sway of the Emperor."
10095-8{_}1809{_}"I know that well, Benedetto," said Manto, "and hence will take good heed not to counsel Mantua to choose thee.
10095-8{_}1810{_}No, the Duke I will give her shall be one without passions to gratify or injuries to avenge, and shall already be crowned with a crown to make the ducal cap as nothing in his eyes, if eyes he had."
10095-8{_}1811{_}Benedetto departed in hot displeasure, and the  alchemist came forward to announce that the commissioners waited.
10095-8{_}1812{_}"My projection," he whispered, "only wants one more piece of gold to insure success, and Eustachio proffers thirty.
10095-8{_}1813{_}Oh, give him Mantua in exchange for boundless riches!"
10095-8{_}1814{_}"And they call thee a philosopher and me a visionary!"
10095-8{_}1815{_}said Manto, patting his cheek.
10095-8{_}1816{_}The envoys' commission having been unfolded, she took not a moment to reply, "Be your Duke Virgil."
10095-8{_}1817{_}The deputation respectfully represented that although Virgil was no doubt Mantua's greatest citizen, he laboured under the disqualification of having been dead more than twelve hundred years.
10095-8{_}1818{_}Nothing further, however, could be extorted from the prophetess, and the ambassadors were obliged to withdraw.
10095-8{_}1819{_}The interpretation of Manto's oracle naturally provoked much diversity of opinion in the council.
10095-8{_}1820{_}"Obviously," said a poet, "the prophetess would have us confer the ducal dignity upon the contemporary bard who doth most nearly accede to the vestiges of the divine Maro; and he, as I judge, is even now in the midst of you."
10095-8{_}1821{_}"Virgil the poet," said a priest, who had long laboured under the suspicion of occult practices, "was a fool to Virgil the enchanter.
10095-8{_}1822{_}The wise woman evidently demands one competent to put the devil into a hole--an operation which I have striven to perform all my life."
10095-8{_}1823{_}"Canst thou balance our city upon an egg?"
10095-8{_}1824{_}inquired Eustachio.
10095-8{_}1825{_}"Better upon an egg than upon a quack!"
10095-8{_}1826{_}retorted the priest.
10095-8{_}1827{_}But such was not the opinion of Eustachio himself, who privately conferred with Leonardo.
10095-8{_}1828{_}Eustachio had a character, but no parts; Leonardo had parts, but no character.
10095-8{_}1829{_}"I see not why these fools should deride the oracle of the prophetess," he said.
10095-8{_}1830{_}"She would doubtless impress upon us that a dead master is in divers respects preferable to a living one."
10095-8{_}1831{_}"Surely," said Eustachio, "provided always that the servant is a man of exemplary character, and that he presumes not upon his lord's withdrawal to another sphere, trusting thereby to commit malpractices with impunity, but doth, on the contrary, deport himself as ever in his great taskmaster's eye."
10095-8{_}1832{_}"Eustachio," said Leonardo, with admiration, "it is the misery of Mantua that she hath no citizen who can act half as well as thou canst talk.
10095-8{_}1833{_}I would fain have further discourse with thee."
10095-8{_}1834{_}The two statesmen laid their heads together, and ere long the mob were crying, "A Virgil!
10095-8{_}1835{_}a Virgil!"
10095-8{_}1836{_}The councillors reassembled and passed resolutions.
10095-8{_}1837{_}"But who shall be Regent?"
10095-8{_}1838{_}inquired some one when Virgil had been elected unanimously.
10095-8{_}1839{_}"Who but we?"
10095-8{_}1840{_}asked Eustachio and Leonardo.
10095-8{_}1841{_}"Are we not the heads of the Virgilian party?"
10095-8{_}1842{_}Thus had the enthusiastic Manto, purest of idealists, installed in authority the two most unprincipled politicians in the republic; and she had lost her lover besides, for Benedetto fled the city, vowing vengeance.
10095-8{_}1843{_}Anyhow, the dead poet was enthroned Duke  of Mantua; Eustachio and Leonardo became Regents, with the style of Consuls, and it was provided that in doubtful cases reference should be made to the Sortes Virgilianae.
10095-8{_}1844{_}And truly, if we may believe the chronicles, the arrangement worked for a time surprisingly well.
10095-8{_}1845{_}The Mantuans, in an irrational way, had done what it behoves all communities to do rationally if they can.
10095-8{_}1846{_}They had sought for a good and worthy citizen to rule them; it was their misfortune that such an one could only be found among the dead.
10095-8{_}1847{_}They felt prouder of themselves for being governed by a great man--one in comparison with whom kings and pontiffs were the creatures of a day.
10095-8{_}1848{_}They would not, if they could help it, disgrace themselves by disgracing their hero; they would not have it said that Mantua, which had not been too weak to bear him, had been too weak to endure his government.
10095-8{_}1849{_}The very hucksters and usurers among them felt dimly that there was such a thing as an Ideal.
10095-8{_}1850{_}A glimmering perception dawned upon mailed, steel-fisted barons that there was such a thing as an Idea, and they felt uneasily apprehensive, like beasts of prey who have for the first time sniffed gunpowder.
10095-8{_}1851{_}The railleries and mockeries of Mantua's neighbours, moreover, stimulated Mantua's citizens to persevere in their course, and deterred them from doing aught to approve themselves fools.
10095-8{_}1852{_}Were not Verona, Cremona, Lodi, Pavia, Crema, cities that could never enthrone the Virgil they had never produced, watching with undissembled expectation to see them trip?
10095-8{_}1853{_}The hollow-hearted Eustachio and the rapacious Leonardo, their virtual rulers, might indeed be little sensible to  this enthusiasm, but they could not disregard the general drift of public opinion, which said clearly: "Mantua is trying a great experiment.
10095-8{_}1854{_}Woe to you if you bring it to nought by your selfish quarrels!"
10095-8{_}1855{_}The best proof that there was something in Manto's idea was that after a while the Emperor Frederick took alarm, and signified to the Mantuans that they must cease their mumming and fooling and acknowledge him as their sovereign, failing which he would besiege their city.
10095-8{_}1856{_}II   Mantua was girt by a zone of fire and steel.
10095-8{_}1857{_}Her villas and homesteads flamed or smoked; her orchards flared heavenward in a torrent of sparks or stood black sapless trunks charred to their inmost pith; the promise of her harvests lay as grey ashes over the land.
10095-8{_}1858{_}But her ramparts, though breached in places, were yet manned by her sons, and their assailants recoiled pierced by the shafts or stunned by the catapults of the defence.
10095-8{_}1859{_}Kaiser Frederick sat in his tent, giving secret audience to one who had stolen in disguise over from the city in the grey of the morning.
10095-8{_}1860{_}By the Emperor's side stood a tall martial figure, wearing a visor which he never removed.
10095-8{_}1861{_}"Your Majesty," Leonardo was saying, for it was he, "this madness will soon pass away.
10095-8{_}1862{_}The people will weary of sacrificing themselves for a dead heathen."
10095-8{_}1863{_}"And Liberty?"
10095-8{_}1864{_}asked the Emperor, "is not that a name dear to those misguided creatures?"
10095-8{_}1865{_}"So dear, please your Majesty, that if they have but the name they will perfectly dispense with the thing.
10095-8{_}1866{_}I do not advise that your imperial yoke should be too palpably adjusted to their stiff necks.
10095-8{_}1867{_}Leave them in appearance the choice of their magistrate, but insure its falling upon one of approved fidelity, certain to execute obsequiously all your Majesty's mandates; such an one, in short, as your faithful vassal Leonardo.
10095-8{_}1868{_}It would only be necessary to decapitate that dangerous revolutionist, Eustachio."
10095-8{_}1869{_}"And the citizens are really ready for this?"
10095-8{_}1870{_}"All the respectable citizens.
10095-8{_}1871{_}All of whom your Majesty need take account.
10095-8{_}1872{_}All men of standing and substance."
10095-8{_}1873{_}"I rejoice to hear it," said the Emperor, "and do the more readily credit thee inasmuch as a most virtuous and honourable citizen hath already been beforehand with thee, assuring me of the same thing, and affirming that but one traitor, whose name, methinks, sounded like thine, stands between me and the subjugation of Mantua."
10095-8{_}1874{_}And, withdrawing a curtain, he disclosed the figure of Eustachio.
10095-8{_}1875{_}"I thought he was asleep," muttered Eustachio.
10095-8{_}1876{_}"That noodle to have been beforehand with me!"
10095-8{_}1877{_}murmured Leonardo.
10095-8{_}1878{_}"What perplexes me," continued Frederick, after enjoying the confusion of the pair for a few moments, "is that our masked friend here will have it that he is the man for the Dukedom, and offers to open the gates to me by a method of his own."
10095-8{_}1879{_}"By fair fighting, an' please my liege," observed  the visored personage, "not by these dastardly treacheries."
10095-8{_}1880{_}"How inhuman!"
10095-8{_}1881{_}sighed Eustachio.
10095-8{_}1882{_}"How old-fashioned!"
10095-8{_}1883{_}sneered Leonardo.
10095-8{_}1884{_}"The truth is," continued Frederick, "he gravely doubts whether either of you possesses the influence which you allege, and has devised a method of putting this to the proof, which I trust will commend itself to you."
10095-8{_}1885{_}Leonardo and Eustachio expressed their readiness to submit their credit with their fellow-citizens to any reasonable trial.
10095-8{_}1886{_}"He proposes, then," pursued the Emperor, "that ye, disarmed and bound, should be placed at the head of the storming column, and in that situation should, as questionless ye would, exert your entire moral influence with your fellow-citizens to dissuade them from shooting you.
10095-8{_}1887{_}If the column, thus shielded, enters the city without resistance, ye will both have earned the Dukedom, and the question who shall have it may be decided by single combat between yourselves.
10095-8{_}1888{_}But should the people, rather than submit to our clemency, impiously slay their elected magistrates, it will be apparent that the methods of our martial friend are the only ones corresponding to the exigency of the case.
10095-8{_}1889{_}Is the storming column ready?"
10095-8{_}1890{_}"All but the first file, please your Majesty," responded the man in the visor.
10095-8{_}1891{_}"Let it be equipped," returned Frederick, and in half-an-hour Eustachio and Leonardo, their hands tied behind them, were stumbling up the breach, impelled by pikes in the rear, and confronting the catapults, _chevaux de frise_, hidden pitfalls, Greek fire, and boiling water provided by  their own direction, and certified to them the preceding evening as all that could be desired.
10095-8{_}1892{_}They had, however, the full use of their voices, and this they turned to the best account.
10095-8{_}1893{_}Never had Leonardo been so cogent, or Eustachio so pathetic.
10095-8{_}1894{_}The Mantuans, already disorganised by the unaccountable disappearance of the Executive, were entirely irresolute what to do.
10095-8{_}1895{_}As they hesitated the visored chief incited his followers.
10095-8{_}1896{_}All seemed lost, when a tall female figure appeared among the defenders.
10095-8{_}1897{_}It was Manto.
10095-8{_}1898{_}"Fools and cowards!"
10095-8{_}1899{_}she exclaimed, "must ye learn your duty from a woman?"
10095-8{_}1900{_}And, seizing a catapult, she discharged a stone which laid the masked warrior stunned and senseless on the ground.
10095-8{_}1901{_}The next instant Eustachio and Leonardo fell dead, pierced by showers of arrows.
10095-8{_}1902{_}The Mantuans sallied forth.
10095-8{_}1903{_}The dismayed Imperialists fled to their camp.
10095-8{_}1904{_}The bodies of the fallen magistrates and of the unconscious chieftain in the mask were brought into the city.
10095-8{_}1905{_}Manto herself undid the fallen man's visor, and uttered a fearful shriek as she recognised Benedetto.
10095-8{_}1906{_}"What shall be done with him, mistress?"
10095-8{_}1907{_}they asked.
10095-8{_}1908{_}Manto long stood silent, torn by conflicting emotions.
10095-8{_}1909{_}At length she said, in a strange, unnatural voice:  "Put him into the Square Tower."
10095-8{_}1910{_}"And now, mistress, what further?
10095-8{_}1911{_}How to choose the new consuls?"
10095-8{_}1912{_}"Ask me no more," she said.
10095-8{_}1913{_}"I shall never prophesy again.
10095-8{_}1914{_}Virtue has gone away from me."
10095-8{_}1915{_}The leaders departed, to intrigue for the vacant  posts, and devise tortures for Benedetto.
10095-8{_}1916{_}Manto sat on the rampart, still and silent as its stones.
10095-8{_}1917{_}Anon she rose, and roved about as if distraught, reciting verses from Virgil.
10095-8{_}1918{_}Night had fallen.
10095-8{_}1919{_}Benedetto lay wakeful in his cell.
10095-8{_}1920{_}A female figure stood before him bearing a lamp.
10095-8{_}1921{_}It was Manto.
10095-8{_}1922{_}"Benedetto," she said, "I am a wretch, faithless to my country and to my master.
10095-8{_}1923{_}I did but even now open his sacred volume at hazard, and on what did my eye first fall?
