10095-8{_}3917{_}_Jupiter_.
10095-8{_}3918{_}It is no less the nature of man to shatter every mirror that does not exhibit to him what he wishes to behold.
10095-8{_}3919{_}_Truth_.
10095-8{_}3920{_}Let me, therefore, return to my well, and let him who wishes to behold me, if such there be, repair to the brink and look down.
10095-8{_}3921{_}_Jupiter_.
10095-8{_}3922{_}No, daughter, you shall not return to your well.
10095-8{_}3923{_}I have already perceived that you are not of yourself sufficient for the office I have assigned to you, and I am about to provide you with two auxiliaries.
10095-8{_}3924{_}You are Truth.
10095-8{_}3925{_}Tell me how this one appears to you.
10095-8{_}3926{_}_Truth_.
10095-8{_}3927{_}Oh, father, the beautiful nymph!
10095-8{_}3928{_}how mature, and yet how comely!
10095-8{_}3929{_}how good-humoured, yet how gentle and grave!
10095-8{_}3930{_}Her robe is closely zoned; her upraised finger approaches her lip; her foot falls soft as snow.
10095-8{_}3931{_}What is her name?
10095-8{_}3932{_}_Jupiter_.
10095-8{_}3933{_}Discretion.
10095-8{_}3934{_}And this other?
10095-8{_}3935{_}_Truth_.
10095-8{_}3936{_}Oh, father!
10095-8{_}3937{_}the cordial look, the blooming cheek, the bright smile that is almost a laugh, the buoyant step, and the expansive bosom!
10095-8{_}3938{_}What name bears she?
10095-8{_}3939{_}_Jupiter_.
10095-8{_}3940{_}Good Nature.
10095-8{_}3941{_}Return, my daughter, to earth; continue to enlighten man's ignorance and to reprove his folly; but let Discretion suggest the occasion, and Good Nature inspire the wording of your admonitions.
10095-8{_}3942{_}I cannot engage that you may not, even with these precautions, sometimes pay a visit to the stake; and if, when an adventure of this sort appears imminent, Discretion should counsel a temporary retirement to your well, I am sure Good Nature will urge nothing to the contrary.
10095-8{_}3943{_}THE THREE PALACES   Three pairs of young people, each a youth with his bride, came together along a road to the point where it divided to the right and left.
10095-8{_}3944{_}On one side was inscribed, "To the Palace of Truth," and on the other, "To the Palace of Illusion."
10095-8{_}3945{_}"This way, my beauty!"
10095-8{_}3946{_}cried one of the youths, drawing his companion in the direction of the Palace of Truth.
10095-8{_}3947{_}"To the place where and where alone thy perfections may be beheld as they are!"
10095-8{_}3948{_}"And my imperfections!"
10095-8{_}3949{_}whispered the young spouse, but her tone was airy and confident.
10095-8{_}3950{_}"Well," said the second youth, "does the choice beseem you upon whom the moon of your nuptials is beaming still.
10095-8{_}3951{_}My beloved and I are riper in Hymen's lore by not less, I ween, than one fortnight.
10095-8{_}3952{_}Prudence impels us towards the Palace of Illusion."
10095-8{_}3953{_}"Thy will is mine, Alonso," said his lady.
10095-8{_}3954{_}"I," said the third youth, "will seek neither; for I would not be wise over-much, while of what I deem myself to know I would be well assured.
10095-8{_}3955{_}Happy am I, and bless my lot, yet have I beheld a red mouse in closer contiguity to my beloved than I could bring myself to approve, albeit it leapt not from her mouth as they do sometimes.
10095-8{_}3956{_}Yet do I know it for a red mouse and nothing worse; had I inhabited the Palace of Illusion  haply I had deemed it a rat.
10095-8{_}3957{_}And, it being a red mouse as it indubitably was, to what end fancy it a tawny-throated nightingale?"
10095-8{_}3958{_}While, therefore, the other pairs proceeded on the paths they had respectively chosen, this sage youth and his bride settled themselves at the parting of the ways, built their cot, tended their garden, tilled their field and raised fruits around them, including children.
10095-8{_}3959{_}The preparation of a cheerful repast was one day well advanced, when, lifting up their eyes, the pair beheld a haggard and emaciated couple tottering along the road that led from the Palace of Illusion.
10095-8{_}3960{_}"Heavens!"
10095-8{_}3961{_}exclaimed they simultaneously, "no!
10095-8{_}3962{_}yes!
10095-8{_}3963{_}'tis surely they!"
10095-8{_}3964{_}O friends!
10095-8{_}3965{_}whence this forlorn semblance?
10095-8{_}3966{_}whence this osseous condition?"
10095-8{_}3967{_}"Of them anon," replied the attenuated youth, "but, before all things, dinner!"
10095-8{_}3968{_}The restorative was speedily administered, and the pilgrim commenced his narration.
10095-8{_}3969{_}"Guarded," he said, "though the Palace of Illusion was by every species of hippogriffic chimaera, my bride and I experienced no difficulty in penetrating inside its precincts.
10095-8{_}3970{_}The giants lifted us in their arms, the dragons carried us on their backs, fairy bridges spanned the moats, golden ladders inclined against the ramparts, we scaled the towers and trod the courts securely, though constructed to all seeming of dissolving cloud.
10095-8{_}3971{_}Delicate fare loaded every dish; smiling companions invited to every festivity; perfumes caressed our nostrils; music enwrapped our ears.
10095-8{_}3972{_}"But while all else charmed and allured, one  fact intruded of which we could not pretend unconsciousness, the intensity of our aversion for each other.
10095-8{_}3973{_}Never could I behold my Imogene without marvelling whatever could have induced me to wed her, and she has acknowledged that she laboured under the like perplexity.
10095-8{_}3974{_}On the other hand, our good opinion of ourselves had grown prodigiously.
10095-8{_}3975{_}The other's dislike appeared to each an insane delusion, and we seriously questioned whether it could be right to mate longer with a being so destitute of true aesthetic feeling.
10095-8{_}3976{_}We confided these scruples to each other, with the result of a most tempestuous altercation.
10095-8{_}3977{_}"As this was attaining its climax, one of the inmates of the Palace, a pert forward boy, resembling a page out of livery, passed by, and ironically, as I thought, congratulated us on the strength of our mutual attachment.
10095-8{_}3978{_}'Never,' exclaimed he, 'have I beheld the like here before, and I am the oldest inhabitant.'
10095-8{_}3979{_}"As this felicitation was proffered at the precise moment when I was engaged in staunching a rent in my cheek with a handful of my wife's hair, I was constrained to regard it as unseasonable, and expressed myself to that effect.
10095-8{_}3980{_}"'What!'
10095-8{_}3981{_}exclaimed he, with equal surprise, 'know ye not that this is the Palace of Illusion, where everything is inverted and appears the reverse of itself?
10095-8{_}3982{_}Intense indeed must be the affection which can thus drive you to fisticuffs!
10095-8{_}3983{_}Had I beheld you billing and cooing, truly I had counselled a judicial separation!'
10095-8{_}3984{_}"My wife and I looked at each other, and by a common impulse made at our utmost speed for the gate of the Palace of Illusion.
10095-8{_}3985{_}"Alas!
10095-8{_}3986{_}it is one thing to enter and another to quit that domain of enchantment.
10095-8{_}3987{_}The golden clouds enwrapt us still, cates and dainties tempted us as of old, the most bewitching strains detained us spellbound.
10095-8{_}3988{_}The giant and dragon warders, indeed, offered no violent resistance, they simply turned into open portals which appeared to yield us egress, but proved entrances to interminable labyrinthine mazes.
10095-8{_}3989{_}At last we escaped by resolutely, following the exact opposite track to that which we observed to be taken by a poet, who was chasing a phantom of Fame with a scroll of unintelligible and inharmonious verse.
10095-8{_}3990{_}"The moment that we emerged from the enchanted castle we knew ourselves and each other for what we were, and fell weeping into each other's arms.
10095-8{_}3991{_}So feeble were we that we could hardly move, nevertheless we have made a shift to crawl hither, trusting to your hospitality to recruit us from the sawdust and ditch-water which we vehemently suspect to have been our diet during the whole of our residence."
10095-8{_}3992{_}"Eat and drink without stint and without ceremony," rejoined their host, "provided only that somewhat remain for the guests whom I see approaching."
10095-8{_}3993{_}And in a few moments the fugitives from the Palace of Illusion were reinforced by travellers from the Palace of Truth, whose backs were most determinately turned to that august edifice.
10095-8{_}3994{_}"My friends," said the youth last arrived, when the first greetings were over, "Truth's Palace might be a not ineligible residence were not the inmates necessitated not merely to know the truth but to speak it, and did not all innocent  embellishments of her majestic person become entirely inefficient and absolutely nugatory.
10095-8{_}3995{_}For example, the number of my wife's grey hairs speedily confounded me; and how should it be otherwise, when the excellent dye she had brought with her had completely lost its virtues?
10095-8{_}3996{_}She on her part found herself continually obliged to acquaint me with the manifold defects she was daily discovering in my mind and person, which I was unable to deny, frequently as I opened my mouth for that purpose.
10095-8{_}3997{_}It is true that I had the satisfaction of pointing out equal defects in herself; but this could not be considered a great satisfaction, seeing that every such discovery impugned my taste and judgment, and impaired the worth of my most cherished possession.
10095-8{_}3998{_}At length we resolved that Truth and we were not made for each other, and, having verified the accuracy of this conclusion by uttering it unrebuked in Truth's own palace, quitted the unblest spot with all possible expedition.
10095-8{_}3999{_}No sooner were we outside than our tenderness revived, and, the rites of reconciliation duly performed, my wife found nothing more urgent than to try whether her dye had recovered its natural properties, which, as ye may perceive, proved to be the case.
10095-8{_}4000{_}We are now bound for the Palace of Illusion."
10095-8{_}4001{_}"Nay," said he who had escaped thence, "if my experience suffices not to deter you, learn that they who have known Truth can never taste of Illusion.
10095-8{_}4002{_}Illusion is for life's golden prime, its fanes and pavilions may be reared but by the magic wand of Youth.
10095-8{_}4003{_}The maturity that would recreate them builds not for Illusion  but for Deceit.
10095-8{_}4004{_}Yet, lest mortality should despair, there exists, as I have learned, yet another palace, founded midway between that of Illusion and that of Truth, open to those who are too soft for the one and too hard for the other.
10095-8{_}4005{_}Thither, indeed, the majority of mankind in this age resort, and there appear to find themselves comfortable."
10095-8{_}4006{_}"And this palace is?"
10095-8{_}4007{_}inquired Truth's runaways simultaneously.
10095-8{_}4008{_}"The Palace of Convention," replied the youth.
10095-8{_}4009{_}NEW READINGS IN BIOGRAPHY    I.--Timon of Athens   No, it was not true that Timon was dead, and buried on the sea-shore.
10095-8{_}4010{_}So the first party discovered that hastened to his cave at the tidings, thinking to seize his treasure, and had their heads broken for their pains.
10095-8{_}4011{_}But the second party fared better; for these were robbers, captained by Alcibiades, who had taken to the road, as many a man of spirit, has done before and since.
10095-8{_}4012{_}They took Timon's gold, and left him bound in his chair.
10095-8{_}4013{_}But on the way home the lesser thieves mysteriously disappeared, and the gold became the sole property of Alcibiades.
10095-8{_}4014{_}As it is written, "The tools to him that can handle them."
10095-8{_}4015{_}Timon sat many hours in an uncomfortable position, and though, in a general way, he abhorred the face of man, he was not displeased when a gentleman of bland appearance entered the cavern, and made him a low obeisance.
10095-8{_}4016{_}And perceiving that Timon was bound, the bland man exclaimed with horror, and severed his bonds, ere one could say Themistocles.
10095-8{_}4017{_}And in an instant the cavern was filled with Athenian senators.
10095-8{_}4018{_}"Hail," they cried, "to Timon the munificent!
10095-8{_}4019{_}Hail to Timon the compassionate!
10095-8{_}4020{_}Hail to Timon the lover of his kind!"
10095-8{_}4021{_}"I am none of these things," said Timon.
10095-8{_}4022{_}"I am Timon the misanthrope."
10095-8{_}4023{_}"This must be my Lord's wit and playfulness," said the bland man, "for how else should the Senate and the people have passed a decree, indited by myself, ordering an altar to be raised to Timon the Benefactor, and appointing him chief archon?
10095-8{_}4024{_}But come, hand over thy treasure, that thy installation may take effect with due observance."
10095-8{_}4025{_}"I have been deprived of my treasure," said Timon.
10095-8{_}4026{_}But the ambassadors gave him no credit until they had searched every chink and crevice in the cavern, and dug up all the earth round the entrance.
10095-8{_}4027{_}They then regarded each other with blank consternation.
10095-8{_}4028{_}"Let us leave him as we found him," said one.
10095-8{_}4029{_}"Let us hang him up," said another.
10095-8{_}4030{_}"Let us sell him into captivity," said a third.
10095-8{_}4031{_}"Nay, friends," said the bland gentleman, "such confession of error would impeach our credit as statesmen.
10095-8{_}4032{_}Moreover, should the people learn that Timon has lost his money, they will naturally conclude that we have taken it.
10095-8{_}4033{_}Let us, therefore, keep this misfortune from their knowledge, and trust for relief to the chapter of accidents, as usual in State affairs."
10095-8{_}4034{_}They therefore robed Timon in a dress of honour, and conducted him to Athens, where half the inhabitants were awaiting him.
10095-8{_}4035{_}Two triumphal arches spanned the principal street, and on one was inscribed "Timon the Benefactor," and  on the other "Timon the Friend of Humanity."
10095-8{_}4036{_}And all along, far as the eye could reach, stood those whom his bounty, as was stated, had rescued from perdition, the poor he had relieved, the sick he had medicined, the orphans he had fathered, the poets and painters he had patronised, all lauding and thanking him, and soliciting a continuance of his liberality.
10095-8{_}4037{_}And the rabble cried "Largesse, largesse!"
10095-8{_}4038{_}and horsemen galloped forth, casting among them nuts enveloped in silver-leaf and apples and comfits and trinkets and brass farthings in incredible quantities.
10095-8{_}4039{_}At which the people murmured somewhat, and spoke amiss respecting Timon and the senators who escorted him, and the bland gentleman strove to keep Timon between himself and the populace.
10095-8{_}4040{_}While Timon was pondering what the end of these things should be, his mob encountered another cheering for Alcibiades, and playing pitch and toss with drachmas and didrachmas and tetradrachmas, yea, even with staters and darics.
10095-8{_}4041{_}"Long live Alcibiades," cried Timon's followers, as they attacked Alcibiades's supporters to get their share.
10095-8{_}4042{_}"Long live Timon," cried Alcibiades's party, as they defended themselves.
10095-8{_}4043{_}Timon and Alcibiades extricated themselves from the scuffle, and walked away arm in arm.
10095-8{_}4044{_}"My dear friend," said Timon, "how inexpressibly beholden I am to you for taking the burden of my wealth upon yourself!
10095-8{_}4045{_}There is nothing I would not do to evince my gratitude."
10095-8{_}4046{_}"Nothing?"
10095-8{_}4047{_}queried Alcibiades.
10095-8{_}4048{_}"Nothing," persisted Timon.
10095-8{_}4049{_}"Then," said Alcibiades, "I will thank thee  to relieve me of Timandra, who is as tired of me as I am of her."
10095-8{_}4050{_}Timon winced horribly, but his word was his bond, and Timandra accompanied him to his cavern, where at first she suffered much inconvenience from the roughness of the accommodation.
10095-8{_}4051{_}But Timon, though a misanthrope, was not a brute; and when in process of time Timandra's health required special care, rugs and pillows were provided for her, and also for Timon; for he saw that he could no longer pass for a churl if he made his wife more comfortable than himself.
10095-8{_}4052{_}And, though he counted gold as dross, yet was he not dissatisfied that Timandra had saved the gold he had given her formerly against a rainy day.
10095-8{_}4053{_}And when a child was born, Timon was at his wits' end, and blessed the old woman who came to nurse it.
10095-8{_}4054{_}And she admonished him of his duty to the Gods, which meant sacrifice, which meant merry-making.
10095-8{_}4055{_}And the child grew, and craved food and drink, and Timon possessed himself of three acres and a cow.
10095-8{_}4056{_}And not being able to doubt his child's affection for him, he came to believe in Timandra's also.
10095-8{_}4057{_}And when the tax-gatherer oppressed his neighbours, he pleaded their cause, which was also his own, in the courts of Athens, and gained it by the interest of Alcibiades.
10095-8{_}4058{_}And his neighbours made him demarch, and he feasted them.
10095-8{_}4059{_}And Apemantus came to deride him, and Timon bore with him; but he was impertinent to Timandra, and Timon beat him.
10095-8{_}4060{_}And in fine, Timon became very like any other Attic country gentleman, save that he always maintained that a young man did well to be a  misanthrope until he got a loving and sensible wife, which, as he observed, could but seldom happen.
10095-8{_}4061{_}And the Gods looked down upon him with complacency, and deferred the ruin of Athens until he should be no more.
10095-8{_}4062{_}II.--Napoleon's Sangaree   Napoleon Buonaparte sat in his garden at St. Helena, in the shadow of a fig-tree.
10095-8{_}4063{_}Before him stood a little table, and upon the table stood a glass of sangaree.
10095-8{_}4064{_}The day was hot and drowsy; the sea boomed monotonously on the rocks; the broad fig-leaves stirred not; great flies buzzed heavily in the sultry air.
10095-8{_}4065{_}Napoleon wore a loose linen coat and a broad brimmed planter's hat, and looked as red as the sangaree, but nowise as comfortable.
10095-8{_}4066{_}"To think," he said aloud, "that I should end my life here, with nothing to sweeten my destiny but this lump of sugar!"
10095-8{_}4067{_}And he dropped it into the sangaree, and little ripples and beads broke out on the surface of the liquid.
10095-8{_}4068{_}"Thou should'st have followed me," said a voice.
10095-8{_}4069{_}"Me," said another.
10095-8{_}4070{_}And a steam from the sangaree rose high over Napoleon's head, and from it shaped themselves two beautiful female figures.
10095-8{_}4071{_}One was fair and very youthful, with a Phrygian cap on her head, and eager eyes beneath it, and a slender spear in her hand.
10095-8{_}4072{_}The other was somewhat older,  and graver, and darker, with serious eyes; and she carried a sword, and wore a helmet, from underneath which her rich brown tresses escaped over her vesture of light steel armour.
10095-8{_}4073{_}"I am Liberty," said the first.
10095-8{_}4074{_}"I am Loyalty," said the second.
10095-8{_}4075{_}And Napoleon laid his hand in that of the first spirit, and instantly saw himself as he had been in the days of his youthful victories, only beset with a multitude of people who were offering him a crown, and cheering loudly.
10095-8{_}4076{_}But he thrust it aside, and they cheered ten times more, and fell into each other's arms, and wept and kissed each other.
10095-8{_}4077{_}And troops of young maidens robed in white danced before him, strewing his way with flowers.
10095-8{_}4078{_}And the debts of the debtor were paid, and the prisoners were released from captivity.
10095-8{_}4079{_}And the forty Academicians came bringing Napoleon the prize of virtue.
10095-8{_}4080{_}And the Abbé Sieyès stood up, and offered Napoleon his choice of seventeen constitutions; and Napoleon chose the worst.
10095-8{_}4081{_}And he came to sit with five hundred other men, mostly advocates.
10095-8{_}4082{_}And when he said "Yea," they said "Nay"; and when he said "white," they said "black."
10095-8{_}4083{_}And they suffered him to do neither good nor evil, and when he went to war they commanded his army for him, until he was smitten with a great slaughter.
10095-8{_}4084{_}And the enemy entered the country, and bread was scarce and wine dear; and the people cursed Napoleon, and Liberty vanished from before him.
10095-8{_}4085{_}But he roamed on, ever looking for her, and at length he found her lying dead in the public way, all gashed and bleeding, and trampled with the feet of men and horses, and the wheel of a tumbril  was over her neck.
10095-8{_}4086{_}And Napoleon, under compulsion of the mob, ascended the tumbril; and Abbé Sieyès and Bishop Talleyrand rode at his side, administering spiritual consolation.
10095-8{_}4087{_}Thus they came within sight of the guillotine, whereon stood M. de Robespierre in his sky-blue coat, and his jaw bound up in a bloody cloth, bowing and smiling, nevertheless, and beckoning Napoleon to ascend to him.
10095-8{_}4088{_}Napoleon had never feared the face of man; but when he saw M. de Robespierre great dread fell upon him, and he leapt out of the tumbril, and fled amain, passing amid the people as it were mid withered leaves, until he came where Loyalty stood awaiting him.
10095-8{_}4089{_}She took his hand in hers, and, lo!
10095-8{_}4090{_}another great host of people proffering him a crown, save one little old man, who alone of them all wore his hair in a queue with powder.
10095-8{_}4091{_}"See," said the little old man, "that thou takest not what doth not belong to thee."
10095-8{_}4092{_}"To whom belongeth it then?"
10095-8{_}4093{_}asked Napoleon, "for I am a plain soldier, and have no skill in politics."
10095-8{_}4094{_}"To Louis the Disesteemed," said the little old man, "for he is a great-great-nephew of the Princess of Schwoffingen, whose ancestors reigned here at the flood."
10095-8{_}4095{_}"Where dwells Louis the Disesteemed?"
10095-8{_}4096{_}asked Napoleon.
10095-8{_}4097{_}"In England," said the little old man.
10095-8{_}4098{_}Napoleon therefore repaired to England, and sought for Louis the Disesteemed.
10095-8{_}4099{_}But none could direct him, save that it behoved him to seek in the obscurest places.
10095-8{_}4100{_}And one day, as he was passing through a mean street, he heard  a voice of lamentation, and perceived a man whose coat and shirt were rent and dirty; but not so his pantaloons, for he had none.
10095-8{_}4101{_}"Who art thou, thou pantaloonless one?"
10095-8{_}4102{_}asked he, "and wherefore makest thou this lamentation?"
10095-8{_}4103{_}"I am Louis the Esteemed, King of France and Navarre," replied the distrousered personage, "and I lament for my pantaloons, which I have been enforced to pawn, inasmuch as the broker would advance nothing upon my coat or my shirt."
10095-8{_}4104{_}And Napoleon went upon his knees and divested himself of his own nether garments, and arrayed the king therein, to the great diversion of those who stood about.
10095-8{_}4105{_}"Thou hast done wickedly," said the king when he heard who Napoleon was, "in that thou hast presumed to fight battles and win victories without any commission from me.
10095-8{_}4106{_}Go, nevertheless, and lose an arm, a leg, and an eye in my service, then shall thy offence be forgiven thee."
10095-8{_}4107{_}And Napoleon raised a great army, and gained a great battle for the king, and lost an arm.
10095-8{_}4108{_}And he gained another greater battle, and lost a leg.
10095-8{_}4109{_}And he gained the greatest battle of all; and the king sat on the throne of his ancestors, and was called Louis the Victorious: but Napoleon had lost an eye.
10095-8{_}4110{_}And he came into the king's presence, bearing his eye, his arm, and his leg.
10095-8{_}4111{_}"Thou art pardoned," said the king, "and I will even confer a singular honour upon thee.
10095-8{_}4112{_}Thou shalt defray the expense of my coronation,  which shall be the most splendid ever seen in France."
10095-8{_}4113{_}So Napoleon lost all his substance, and no man pitied him.
10095-8{_}4114{_}But after certain days the keeper of the royal wardrobe rushed into the king's presence, crying "Treason!
10095-8{_}4115{_}treason!
10095-8{_}4116{_}O Majesty, whence these republican and revolutionary pantaloons?"
10095-8{_}4117{_}"They are those I deigned to receive from the rebel Buonaparte," said the king.
10095-8{_}4118{_}"It were meet to return them.
10095-8{_}4119{_}Where abides he now?"
10095-8{_}4120{_}"Saving your Majesty's presence," they said, "he lieth upon a certain dunghill."
10095-8{_}4121{_}"If this be so," said the king, "life can be no gratification to him, and it were humane to relieve him of it.
10095-8{_}4122{_}Moreover, he is a dangerous man.
10095-8{_}4123{_}Go, therefore, and strangle him with his own pantaloons.
10095-8{_}4124{_}Yet, let a monument be raised to him, and engrave upon it, 'Here lies Napoleon Buonaparte, whom Louis the Victorious raised from the dunghill.'"
10095-8{_}4125{_}They went accordingly; but behold!
10095-8{_}4126{_}Napoleon already lay dead upon the dunghill.
10095-8{_}4127{_}And this was told unto the king.
10095-8{_}4128{_}"He hath ever been envious of my glory," said the king, "let him therefore be buried underneath."
10095-8{_}4129{_}And it was so.
10095-8{_}4130{_}And after no long space the king also died, and slept with his fathers.
10095-8{_}4131{_}But when there was again a revolution in France, the people cast his bones out of the royal sepulchre, and laid Napoleon's there instead.
10095-8{_}4132{_}And the dunghill complained grievously that it should be disturbed for so slight a cause.
10095-8{_}4133{_}And Napoleon withdrew his hand from the  hand of Loyalty, saying, "Pish!"
10095-8{_}4134{_}And his eyes opened, and he heard the booming of the sea, and the buzzing of the flies, and felt the heat of the sun, and saw that the sugar he had dropped into his sangaree had not yet reached the bottom of the tumbler.
10095-8{_}4135{_}III.--Concerning Daniel Defoe   Daniel Defoe, at the invitation of the judge, came forth from the garret wherein he abode, and rode in a cart unto the Royal Exchange, wherein he ascended the pillory, to the end that his ears might be nailed thereunto.
10095-8{_}4136{_}And much people stood before him, some few pelting, some mocking, but the most part cheering or weeping, for they knew him for a friend to the poor, and especially those men who were called Dissenters.
10095-8{_}4137{_}And a certain person in black stood by him, invisible to the people, but well seen of Daniel, who knew him for one whose life he had himself written.
10095-8{_}4138{_}And the man in black reasoned with Daniel, and said, "Thou seest this multitude of people, but which of them shall deliver thee out of my hand?
10095-8{_}4139{_}Nay, but let thy white be black, and thy black white, and I myself will deliver thee, and make thee rich, and heal thy hurts, save the holes in thy ears, that I may know thee for mine own."
10095-8{_}4140{_}But Daniel gave no heed to him.
10095-8{_}4141{_}So the Devil departed, having great wrath, and entered into a certain smug-faced man standing by.
10095-8{_}4142{_}And now the crowd before Daniel was greatly  diminished, and consisted mainly of his enemies, for his friends had gone away to drown their sorrow.
10095-8{_}4143{_}And the smug-faced man into whom Satan had entered came forth from among them, and said unto him, "O Daniel, inasmuch as I am a Dissenter I am greatly beholden to thee; but inasmuch as I am an honest tradesman I have somewhat against thee, for thou hast written concerning short weights and measures.
10095-8{_}4144{_}And a man's shop is more to him than his country or his religion.
10095-8{_}4145{_}Wherefore I must needs be avenged of thee.
10095-8{_}4146{_}Yet shalt thou own that the tender mercies of the good man are piteous, and that even in his wrath he thinketh upon compassion."
10095-8{_}4147{_}And he picked up a great stone from the ground, and wrapped it in a piece of paper, saying, "Lest peradventure it hurt him overmuch."
10095-8{_}4148{_}And the stone was very rough and sharp, and the paper was very thin.
10095-8{_}4149{_}And he hurled it with all his might at the middle of Daniel's forehead, and the blood spouted forth.
10095-8{_}4150{_}And Daniel cried aloud, and called upon the name of the Devil.
10095-8{_}4151{_}And in an instant the pillory and the people were gone, and he found himself in the Prime Minister's cabinet, healed of all his hurts, except the holes in his ears.
10095-8{_}4152{_}And the Minister was so like the Devil that you could not tell the difference.
10095-8{_}4153{_}And he said, "Against what wilt thou write first, Daniel?"
10095-8{_}4154{_}"Dissenters," said Daniel.
10095-8{_}4155{_}And he wrote a pamphlet, and such as read it took firebrands, and visited the Dissenters in their habitations.
10095-8{_}4156{_}And many Dissenters were put into prison, and others fined and spoiled of  their goods.
10095-8{_}4157{_}And he wrote other pamphlets, and each was cleverer and wickeder than the last.
10095-8{_}4158{_}And whatsoever Daniel had of old declared to be white, lo!
10095-8{_}4159{_}it was black; and what he had said was black, behold!
10095-8{_}4160{_}it was white.
10095-8{_}4161{_}And he throve and prospered exceedingly, and became a commissioner for public-houses and hackney-coaches and the imposing of oaths and the levying of custom, and all other such things as one does by deputy.
10095-8{_}4162{_}And he mended the holes in his ears.
10095-8{_}4163{_}But the time came when Daniel must be judged, and he went before the Lord.
10095-8{_}4164{_}And all the court was full of Dissenters, and the Devil was there also.
10095-8{_}4165{_}And the Dissenters testified many and grievous things against Daniel.
10095-8{_}4166{_}"Daniel," said the Lord, "what answerest thou?"
10095-8{_}4167{_}"Nothing, Lord," said Daniel.
10095-8{_}4168{_}"Only I would that the Dissenter who threw that stone at me should receive due and condign punishment, adequate to his misdeed."
10095-8{_}4169{_}"That," said the Devil, "is impossible."
10095-8{_}4170{_}"Thou sayest well, Satan," said the Lord, "and therefore shall Daniel go free.
10095-8{_}4171{_}For if anything can excuse the apostasy of the noble, it is the ingratitude of the base."
10095-8{_}4172{_}So the Devil went to his own place, looking very small.
10095-8{_}4173{_}And Daniel found himself in the same garret whence he had gone forth to the pillory; and before him were bread and cheese, and a pen and ink and paper.
10095-8{_}4174{_}And he dipped the pen into the ink, and wrote _Robinson Crusoe_.
10095-8{_}4175{_}IV.--Cornelius the Ferryman   Fourscore years ago there was a good ferryman named Cornelius, who rowed people between New York and Brooklyn.
10095-8{_}4176{_}He had neither wife nor child, nor any one to think of except himself.
10095-8{_}4177{_}It was, therefore, his custom, when he had earned enough in a day for his own wants, to put the rest aside, and bestow it upon sick or blind or maimed persons, lest they should come to the workhouse.
10095-8{_}4178{_}And the sick and the blind and the maimed gathered around him, and waited by the water's edge, until Cornelius's day's work should be over.
10095-8{_}4179{_}This went on until one of the little sooty imps who are always in mischief came to hear of it, and told the principal devil in charge of the United States, whose name is Politicianus.
10095-8{_}4180{_}"Dear me," said the Devil, "this will never do.
10095-8{_}4181{_}I will see to it immediately."
10095-8{_}4182{_}And he went off to Cornelius, and caught him in the act of giving two dimes to a blind beggar.
10095-8{_}4183{_}"How foolish you are!"
10095-8{_}4184{_}he said; "what waste of money is this!
10095-8{_}4185{_}If you saved it up, you would by-and-by be able to build an hospital for all the beggars in New York."
10095-8{_}4186{_}"It would be a long time before there was enough," objected Cornelius.
10095-8{_}4187{_}"Not at all," said the Devil, "if you let me invest your money for you."
10095-8{_}4188{_}And he showed Cornelius the plan of a most splendid hospital, and across the front of it was inscribed in letters of gold, _Cornelius Diabolodorus_.
10095-8{_}4189{_}And Cornelius  was persuaded, and that evening he gave nothing to the poor.
10095-8{_}4190{_}And the poor had come to think that Cornelius's money was their own, and abused him as though he had robbed them.
10095-8{_}4191{_}And Cornelius drove them away: and his heart was hardened against them from that day forth.
10095-8{_}4192{_}But the Devil kept his promise to Cornelius, and put him up to all the good things in Wall Street, and he soon had enough to build ten hospitals.
10095-8{_}4193{_}But the more he had to build with, the less he wanted to build.
10095-8{_}4194{_}And by-and-by the Devil called upon him, and found him contemplating two pictures.
10095-8{_}4195{_}One of them showed the finest hospital you can imagine, full of neat, clean rooms, in one of which sat Cornelius himself, wearing a dress with a number and badge, and sipping arrowroot.
10095-8{_}4196{_}The other showed fine houses, and opera-boxes, and fast-trotting horses, and dry champagne, and ladies who dance in ballets, and paintings by the great masters.
10095-8{_}4197{_}Cornelius thrust the pictures away, and the Devil did not ask to see them, nor was it needful that he should, for he had painted them himself.
10095-8{_}4198{_}"O dear Mr. Devil," said Cornelius, "I am so glad that you have called, for I wanted to speak to you.
10095-8{_}4199{_}It strikes me that there is a great defect in the plan which you have been so good as to draw for me."
10095-8{_}4200{_}"What is that?"
10095-8{_}4201{_}asked the Devil.
10095-8{_}4202{_}"There is no place for black men," said Cornelius.
10095-8{_}4203{_}"And you know white men will never let them come into the same hospital."
10095-8{_}4204{_}And the Devil, to do him justice, talked very reasonably to Cornelius, and represented to him that there were very few black men in New  York, and that these had very vigorous constitutions.
10095-8{_}4205{_}But Cornelius was inflamed with enthusiasm, and frantic with philanthropy, and he vowed that he would not give a cent to an hospital that had not a wing for black men as big as all the rest of the building.
10095-8{_}4206{_}And the Devil had to take his plan back, and come again in a year and a day.
10095-8{_}4207{_}And when he did come back, Cornelius asked him if he did not think it would be a most excellent thing if all the Irishmen in New York could be shut up in an hospital or elsewhere; and he could not deny it.
10095-8{_}4208{_}So he had to take his plan back again.
10095-8{_}4209{_}And next year it was the turn of the Chinese, and then of the Red Indians, and then of the dogs and cats.
10095-8{_}4210{_}And then Cornelius thought that he ought to provide room for all the people who had been ruined by his speculations, and the Devil thought so too, but doubted whether Cornelius would be able to afford it.
10095-8{_}4211{_}And at last Cornelius said:  "Methinks I have been very foolish in wishing to build an hospital at all while I am living.
10095-8{_}4212{_}Surely it would be better that I should enjoy my money myself during my life, and leave the residue for the lawyers to divide after my death."
10095-8{_}4213{_}"You are quite right," said the Devil; "that is exactly what I should do if I were you."
10095-8{_}4214{_}So Cornelius put the plans behind a shelf in his counting-house, and the mice ate them.
10095-8{_}4215{_}And he went on prospering and growing rich, until the Devil became envious of him, and insisted on changing places with him.
10095-8{_}4216{_}So Cornelius went below, and the Devil came and dwelt in New York, where he still is.
10095-8{_}4217{_}THE POISON MAID          O not for him   Blooms my dark nightshade, nor doth hemlock brew   Murder for cups within her cavernous root.
10095-8{_}4218{_}I   Grievous is the lot of the child, more especially of the female child, who is doomed from the tenderest infancy to lack the blessing of a mother's care.
10095-8{_}4219{_}Was it from this absence of maternal vigilance that the education of the lovely Mithridata was conducted from her babyhood in such an extraordinary manner?
10095-8{_}4220{_}That enormous serpents infested her cradle, licking her face and twining around her limbs?
10095-8{_}4221{_}That her tiny fingers patted scorpions?
10095-8{_}4222{_}and tied knots in the tails of vipers?
10095-8{_}4223{_}That her father, the magician Locuste, ever sedulous and affectionate, fed her with spoonsful of the honeyed froth that gathers under the tongues of asps?
10095-8{_}4224{_}That as she grew older and craved a more nutritious diet, she partook, at first in infinitesimal doses, but in ever increasing quantities, of arsenic, strychnine, opium, and prussic acid?
10095-8{_}4225{_}That at last having attained the flower of youth, she drank habitually from vessels of gold, for her favourite beverages were so corrosive that no other substance could resist their solvent properties?
10095-8{_}4226{_}Gradually accustomed to this strange regimen, she had thriven on it marvellously, and was without a peer for beauty, sense, and goodness.
10095-8{_}4227{_}Her father had watched over her education with care, and had instructed her in all lawful knowledge, save only the knowledge of poisons.
10095-8{_}4228{_}As no other human being had entered the house, Mithridata was unaware that her bringing up had differed in so material a respect from that of other young people.
10095-8{_}4229{_}"Father," said she one day, bringing him a book she had been perusing, "what strange follies learned men will pen with gravity!
10095-8{_}4230{_}or is it rather that none can set bounds to the licence of romancers?
10095-8{_}4231{_}These dear serpents, my friends and playfellows, this henbane and antimony, the nourishment of my health and vigour--that any one should write of these as pernicious, deadly, and fatal to existence!
10095-8{_}4232{_}Is it error or malignity?
10095-8{_}4233{_}or but the wanton freak of an idle imagination?"
10095-8{_}4234{_}"My child," answered the magician, "it is fit that thou shouldst now learn what hath hitherto been concealed from thee, and with this object I left this treatise in thy way.
10095-8{_}4235{_}It speaks truth.
10095-8{_}4236{_}Thou hast been nurtured from thy infancy on substances endowed with lethal properties, commonly called poisons.
10095-8{_}4237{_}Thy entire frame is impregnated thereby, and, although thou thyself art in the fullest enjoyment of health, thy kiss would be fatal to any one not, like thy father, fortified by a course of antidotes.
10095-8{_}4238{_}Now hear the reason.
10095-8{_}4239{_}I bear a deadly grudge to the king of this land.
10095-8{_}4240{_}He indeed hath not injured me; but his father slew my father, wherefore it is meet that I should slay that ancestor's son's son.
10095-8{_}4241{_}I have therefore nurtured thee from thy infancy on  the deadliest poisons, until thou art a walking vial of pestilence.
10095-8{_}4242{_}The young prince shall unseal thee, to his destruction and thy unspeakable advantage.
10095-8{_}4243{_}Go to the great city; thou art beautiful as the day; he is young, handsome, and amorous; he will infallibly fall in love with thee.
10095-8{_}4244{_}Do thou submit to his caresses, he will perish miserably; thou (such is the charm) ransomed by the kiss of love, wilt become wholesome and innocuous as thy fellows, preserving only thy knowledge of poisons, always useful, in the present state of society invaluable.
10095-8{_}4245{_}Thou wilt therefore next repair to the city of Constantinople, bearing recommendatory letters from me to the Empress Theophano, now happily reigning."
10095-8{_}4246{_}"Father," said Mithridata, "either I shall love this young prince, or I shall not.
10095-8{_}4247{_}If I do not love him, I am nowise minded to suffer him to caress me.
10095-8{_}4248{_}If I do love him, I am as little minded to be the cause of his death."
10095-8{_}4249{_}"Not even in consideration of the benefit which will accrue to thee by this event?"
10095-8{_}4250{_}"Not even for that consideration."
10095-8{_}4251{_}"O these daughters!"
10095-8{_}4252{_}exclaimed the old man.
10095-8{_}4253{_}"We bring them up tenderly, we exhaust all our science for the improvement of their minds and bodies, we set our choicest hopes upon them, and entrust them with the fulfilment of our most cherished aspirations; and when all is done, they will not so much as commit a murder to please us!
10095-8{_}4254{_}Miserable ingrate, receive the just requital of thy selfish disobedience!"
10095-8{_}4255{_}"O father, do not turn me into a tadpole!"
10095-8{_}4256{_}"I will not, but I will turn thee out of doors."
10095-8{_}4257{_}And he did.
10095-8{_}4258{_}II   Though disinherited, Mithridata was not destitute.
10095-8{_}4259{_}She had secured a particle of the philosopher's stone--a slender outfit for a magician's daughter!
10095-8{_}4260{_}yet ensuring her a certain portion of wealth.
10095-8{_}4261{_}What should she do now?
10095-8{_}4262{_}The great object of her life must henceforth be to avoid committing murder, especially murdering any handsome young man.
10095-8{_}4263{_}It would have seemed most natural to retire into a convent, but, not to speak of her lack of vocation, she felt that her father would justly consider that she had disgraced her family, and she still looked forward to reconciliation with him.
10095-8{_}4264{_}She might have taken a hermitage, but her instinct told her that a fair solitary can only keep young men off by strong measures; and she disliked the character of a hermitess with a bull-dog.
10095-8{_}4265{_}She therefore went straight to the great city, took a house, and surrounded herself with attendants.
10095-8{_}4266{_}In the choice of these she was particularly careful to select those only whose personal appearance was such as to discourage any approach to familiarity or endearment.
10095-8{_}4267{_}Never before or since was youthful beauty surrounded by such moustached duennas, squinting chambermaids, hunchbacked pages, and stumpy maids-of-all-work.
10095-8{_}4268{_}This was a real sorrow to her, for she loved beauty; it was a still sadder trial that she could no longer feel it right to indulge herself in the least morsel of arsenic; she sighed for strychnia, and pined for prussic acid.
10095-8{_}4269{_}The change of diet was of course at first  most trying to her health, and in fact occasioned a serious illness, but youth and a sound constitution pulled her through.
10095-8{_}4270{_}Reader, hast thou known what it is to live with a heart inflamed by love for thy fellow-creatures which thou couldst manifest neither by word nor deed?
10095-8{_}4271{_}To pine with fruitless longings for good?
10095-8{_}4272{_}and to consume with vain yearnings for usefulness?
10095-8{_}4273{_}To be misjudged and haply reviled by thy fellows for failing to do what it is not given thee to do?
10095-8{_}4274{_}If so, thou wilt pity poor Mithridata, whose nature was most ardent, expansive, and affectionate, but who, from the necessity under which she laboured of avoiding as much as possible all contact with human beings, saw herself condemned to a life of solitude, and knew that she was regarded as a monster of pride and exclusiveness.
10095-8{_}4275{_}She dared bestow no kind look, no encouraging gesture on any one, lest this small beginning should lead to the manifestation of her fatal power.
10095-8{_}4276{_}Her own servants, whose minds were generally as deformed as their bodies, hated her, and bitterly resented what they deemed her haughty disdain of them.
10095-8{_}4277{_}Her munificence none could deny, but bounty without tenderness receives no more gratitude than it deserves.
10095-8{_}4278{_}The young of her own sex secretly rejoiced at her unamiability, regarding it as a providential set-off against her beauty, while they detested and denounced her as a--well, they would say viper in the manger, who spoiled everybody else's lovers and would have none of her own.
10095-8{_}4279{_}For with all Mithridata's severity, there was no getting rid of the young men, the giddy moths that flew around her brilliant but baleful candle.
10095-8{_}4280{_}Not all  the cold water thrown upon them, literally as well as figuratively, could keep them from her door.
10095-8{_}4281{_}They filled her house with bouquets and billets doux; they stood before the windows, they sat on the steps, they ran beside her litter when she was carried abroad, they assembled at night to serenade her, fighting desperately among themselves.
10095-8{_}4282{_}They sought to gain admission as tradesmen, as errand boys, even as scullions male and female.
10095-8{_}4283{_}To such lengths did they proceed, that a particularly audacious youth actually attempted to carry her off one evening, and would have succeeded but for the interposition of another, who flew at him with a drawn sword, and after a fierce contest smote him bleeding to the ground.
10095-8{_}4284{_}Mithridata had fainted, of course.
10095-8{_}4285{_}What was her horror on reviving to find herself in the arms of a young man of exquisite beauty and princely mien, sucking death from her lips with extraordinary relish!
10095-8{_}4286{_}She shrieked, she struggled; if she made any unfeminine use of her hands, let the urgency of the case plead her apology.
10095-8{_}4287{_}The youth reproached her bitterly for her ingratitude.
10095-8{_}4288{_}She listened in silent misery, unable to defend herself.
10095-8{_}4289{_}The shaft of love had penetrated her bosom also, and it cost her almost as much for her own sake to dismiss the young man as it did to see him move away, slowly and languidly staggering to his doom.
10095-8{_}4290{_}For the next few days messages came continually, urging her to haste to a youth dying for her sake, whom her presence would revive effectually.
10095-8{_}4291{_}She steadily refused, but how much her refusal cost her!
10095-8{_}4292{_}She wept, she wrung her hands, she called for death and execrated her nurture.
10095-8{_}4293{_}With that strange appetite for self-torment which almost seems to diminish the pangs of the wretched, she collected books on poisons, studied all the symptoms described, and fancied her hapless lover undergoing them all in turn.
10095-8{_}4294{_}At length a message came which admitted of no evasion.
10095-8{_}4295{_}The King commanded her presence.
10095-8{_}4296{_}Admonished by past experience, she provided herself with a veil and mask, and repaired to the palace.
10095-8{_}4297{_}The old King seemed labouring under deep affliction; under happier circumstances he must have been joyous and debonair.
10095-8{_}4298{_}He addressed her with austerity, yet with kindness.
10095-8{_}4299{_}"Maiden," he began, "thy unaccountable cruelty to my son----"  "Thy son!"
10095-8{_}4300{_}she exclaimed, "The Prince!
10095-8{_}4301{_}O father, thou art avenged for my disobedience!"
10095-8{_}4302{_}"Surpasses what history hath hitherto recorded of the most obdurate monsters.
10095-8{_}4303{_}Thou art indebted to him for thy honour, to preserve which he has risked his life.
10095-8{_}4304{_}Thou bringest him to the verge of the grave by thy cruelty, and when a smile, a look from thee would restore him, thou wilt not bestow it."
10095-8{_}4305{_}"Alas!
10095-8{_}4306{_}great King," she replied, "I know too well what your Majesty's opinion of me must be.
10095-8{_}4307{_}I must bear it as I may.
10095-8{_}4308{_}Believe me, the sight of me could effect nothing towards the restoration of thy son."
10095-8{_}4309{_}"Of that I shall judge," said the King, "when thou hast divested thyself of that veil and mask."
10095-8{_}4310{_}Mithridata reluctantly complied.
10095-8{_}4311{_}"By Heaven!"
10095-8{_}4312{_}exclaimed the King, "such a  sight might recall the departing soul from Paradise.
10095-8{_}4313{_}Haste to my son, and instantly; it is not yet too late."
10095-8{_}4314{_}"O King," urged Mithridata, "how could this countenance do thy son any good?
10095-8{_}4315{_}Is he not suffering from the effects of seventy-two poisons?"
10095-8{_}4316{_}"I am not aware of that," said the King.
10095-8{_}4317{_}"Are not his entrails burned up with fire?
10095-8{_}4318{_}Is not his flesh in a state of deliquescence?
10095-8{_}4319{_}Has not his skin already peeled off his body?
10095-8{_}4320{_}Is he not tormented by incessant gripes and vomitings?"
10095-8{_}4321{_}"Not to my knowledge," said the King.
10095-8{_}4322{_}"The symptoms, as I understand, are not unlike those which I remember to have experienced myself, in a milder form, certainly.
10095-8{_}4323{_}He lies in bed, eats and drinks nothing, and incessantly calls upon thee."
10095-8{_}4324{_}"This is most incomprehensible," said Mithridata.
10095-8{_}4325{_}"There was no drug in my father's laboratory that could have produced such an effect."
10095-8{_}4326{_}"The sum of the matter is," continued the King, "that either thou wilt repair forthwith to my son's chamber, and subsequently to church; or else unto the scaffold."
10095-8{_}4327{_}"If it must be so, I choose the scaffold," said Mithridata resolutely.
10095-8{_}4328{_}"Believe me, O King, my appearance in thy son's chamber would but destroy whatever feeble hope of recovery may remain.
10095-8{_}4329{_}I love him beyond everything on earth, and not for worlds would I have his blood on my soul."
10095-8{_}4330{_}"Chamberlain," cried the monarch, "bring me a strait waistcoat."
10095-8{_}4331{_}Driven into a corner, Mithridata flung herself at the King's feet, taking care, however, not to touch him, and confided to him all her wretched history.
10095-8{_}4332{_}The venerable monarch burst into a peal of laughter.
10095-8{_}4333{_}"À bon chat bon rat!"
10095-8{_}4334{_}he exclaimed, as soon as he had recovered himself.
10095-8{_}4335{_}"So thou art the daughter of my old friend the magician Locusto!
10095-8{_}4336{_}I fathomed his craft, and, as he fed his child upon poisons, I fed mine upon antidotes.
10095-8{_}4337{_}Never did any child in the world take an equal quantity of physic: but there is now no poison on earth can harm him.
10095-8{_}4338{_}Ye are clearly made for each other; haste to his bedside, and, as the spell requires, rid thyself of thy venefic properties in his arms as expeditiously as possible.
10095-8{_}4339{_}Thy father shall be bidden to the wedding, and an honoured guest he shall be, for having taught us that the kiss of Love is the remedy for every poison."
10095-8{_}4340{_}NOTES   The first edition of these Tales was published in 1888.
10095-8{_}4341{_}It contained sixteen stories, to which twelve are added in the present impression.
10095-8{_}4342{_}Many originally appeared in periodicals, as will be found indicated in the annotations which the recondite character of some allusions has rendered it desirable to append, and which further provide an opportunity of tendering thanks to many friends for their assent to republication.
10095-8{_}4343{_}P. 5.
10095-8{_}4344{_}_The divine tongue of Greece was forgotten,_--Hereby we may detect the error of those among the learned who have identified Caucasia with Armenia.
10095-8{_}4345{_}"Hellenic letters," says Mr. Capes, writing of Armenia in the fourth century, "were welcomed with enthusiasm, and young men of the slenderest means crowded to the schools of Athens" ("University Life in Ancient Athens," p. 73).
10095-8{_}4346{_}P. 28.
10095-8{_}4347{_}_Who have discovered the Elixir of Immortality._--The belief in this elixir was general in China about the seventh century, A.D., and many emperors used great exertions to discover it.
10095-8{_}4348{_}This fact forms the groundwork of Leopold Schefer's novel, "Der Unsterblichkeitstrank," which has furnished the conception, though not the incidents, of "The Potion of Lao-Tsze."
10095-8{_}4349{_}P. 38.
10095-8{_}4350{_}_So she took the sceptre, and reigned gloriously._--In A.D. 683, the Dowager-Empress Woo How, upon her husband's death, caused her son to be set aside, and ruled prosperously until her decease in 703.
10095-8{_}4351{_}In our day we have seen China virtually governed by female sovereigns.
10095-8{_}4352{_}P. 50.
10095-8{_}4353{_}_Ananda the Miracle Worker._--This story was originally published in Fraser's Magazine for August, 1872.
10095-8{_}4354{_}A French translation appeared in the _Revue Britannique_ for November, 1872.
10095-8{_}4355{_}Buddha's prohibition to work miracles rests, so far as the present writer's knowledge extends, on the authority of Professor Max Müller ("Lectures on the  Science of Religion").
10095-8{_}4356{_}It should be needless to observe that Ananda, "the St. John of the Buddhist group," is not recorded to have contravened this or any other of his master's precepts.
10095-8{_}4357{_}P. 66.
10095-8{_}4358{_}_The City of Philosophers._--This story has been translated into French by M. Sarrazin.
10095-8{_}4359{_}P. 68.
10095-8{_}4360{_}_There to establish a philosophic commonwealth._--The petition was actually preferred, and would have been granted but for the disordered condition of the empire.
10095-8{_}4361{_}Gallienus, though not the man to save a sinking state, possessed the accomplishments which would have adorned an age of peace and culture.
10095-8{_}4362{_}P. 82.
10095-8{_}4363{_}_The sword doubled up; it had neither point nor edge._--Gallienus was fond of such practical jocularity.
10095-8{_}4364{_}"Quum quidam gemmas vitreas pro veris vendiderat ejus uxori, atque illa, re prodita, vindicari vellet, surripi quasi ad leonem venditorem jussit.
10095-8{_}4365{_}Deinde e cavea caponem emittit, mirantibusque cunctis rem tam ridiculam, per curionem dici jussit, 'Imposturam fecit et passus est': deinde negotiatorem dimisit" (Trebellius in Gallieno, cap.
10095-8{_}4366{_}xii.).
10095-8{_}4367{_}P. 100.
10095-8{_}4368{_}_Hypati, anthypati, &c._--_Hypati_ and _anthypati_ denote consuls and proconsuls, dignities of course merely titular at the court of Constantinople.
10095-8{_}4369{_}_Silentiarii_ were properly officers charged with maintaining order at court; but this duty, which was perhaps performed by deputy, seems to have been generally entrusted to persons of distinction.
10095-8{_}4370{_}The _protospatharius_ was the chief of the Imperial body-guard, of which the _spatharocandidati_ constituted the _élite_.
10095-8{_}4371{_}P. 114.
10095-8{_}4372{_}_The Wisdom of the Indians._--Appeared in 1890 in _The Universal Review_.
10095-8{_}4373{_}The idea was suggested by an incident in Dr. Bastian's travels in Burma.
10095-8{_}4374{_}P. 124.
10095-8{_}4375{_}_The Dumb Oracle._--Appeared in the _University Magazine_ for June, 1878.
10095-8{_}4376{_}The legend on which it is founded, a mediaeval myth here transferred to classical times, is also the groundwork of Browning's ballad, "The Boy and the Angel."
10095-8{_}4377{_}P. 136.
10095-8{_}4378{_}_Duke Virgil._--The subject of this story is derived from Leopold Schefer's novel, "Die Sibylle von Mantua," though there is but little resemblance in the incidents.
10095-8{_}4379{_}Schefer cites Friedrich von Quandt as his authority for the Mantuans having actually elected Virgil as their duke in the thirteenth century: but the notion  seems merely founded upon the interpretation of the insignia accompanying a mediæval statue of the poet.
10095-8{_}4380{_}P. 138.
10095-8{_}4381{_}_To put the devil into a hole_.--"Then sayd Virgilius, 'Shulde ye well passe in to the hole that ye cam out of?'
10095-8{_}4382{_}'Yea, I shall well,' sayd the devyl.
10095-8{_}4383{_}'I holde the best plegge that I have, that ye shall not do it.'
10095-8{_}4384{_}'Well,' sayd the devyll, 'thereto I consent.'
10095-8{_}4385{_}And then the devyll wrange himselfe into the lytyll hole ageyne, and he was therein.
10095-8{_}4386{_}Virgilius kyvered the hole ageyne with the borde close, and so was the devyll begyled, and myght nat there come out agen, but abideth shutte still therein" ("Romance of Virgilius").
10095-8{_}4387{_}_Ibid.
10095-8{_}4388{_}Canst thou balance our city upon an egg?_--"Than he thought in his mynde to founde in the middle of the sea a fayre towne, with great landes belongynge to it, and so he did by his cunnynge, and called it Napells.
10095-8{_}4389{_}And the foundacyon of it was of eggs" ("Romance of Virgilius").

10095-8{_}4393{_}_Peter of Abano_.--Pietro di Abano, who took his name from his birthplace, a village near Padua, was a physician contemporary with Dante, whose skill in medicine and astrology caused him to be accused of magic.
10095-8{_}4394{_}It is nevertheless untrue that he was burned by the Inquisition or stoned by the populace; but after his death he was burned in effigy, his remains having been secretly removed by his friends.
10095-8{_}4395{_}Honours were afterwards paid to his memory; and there seems no doubt that he was a man of great attainments, including a knowledge of Greek, and of unblemished character, if he had not sometimes sold his skill at too high a rate.
10095-8{_}4396{_}For his authentic history, see the article in the _Biographie Universelle_ by Ginguené; for the legendary, Tieck's romantic tale, "Pietro von Abano" (1825), which has been translated into English.
10095-8{_}4397{_}P. 156.
10095-8{_}4398{_}_Alexander the Rat-catcher_.--This story, to whose ground-work History and Rabelais have equally contributed, was first published in vol.
10095-8{_}4399{_}xii.
10095-8{_}4400{_}of _The Yellow Book_, January, 1897.
10095-8{_}4401{_}P. 157.
10095-8{_}4402{_}_Cardinal Barbadico_.--This cardinal was actually entrusted by Alexander VIII.
10095-8{_}4403{_}with the commission of suppressing the rats; an occasion upon which the "sardonic grin" imputed to the Pope by a detractor may be conjectured to have been particularly apparent.
10095-8{_}4404{_}Barbadico was a remarkable instance of a man "kicked upstairs."
10095-8{_}4405{_}As  Archbishop of Corfu he had had a violent dispute with the Venetian governor, and Innocent XI., equally unwilling to disown the representative of Papal authority or offend the Republic, recalled him to Rome and made him a Cardinal to keep him there.

10095-8{_}4415{_}_The purple of the emperor and the matrons appeared ashy grey in comparison._ "Cineris specie decolorari videbantur caeterae divini comparatione fulgoris" (Vopiscus, in Vita Aureliani, cap.
10095-8{_}4416{_}xxix.).
10095-8{_}4417{_}P. 230.
10095-8{_}4418{_}_All these sovereigns._--"Diligentissime et Aurelianus et Probus et proxime Diocletianus missis diligentissimis confectoribus requisiverunt tale genus purpurae, nec tamen invenire potuerunt" (Vopiscus, _loc.
10095-8{_}4419{_}cit._).
10095-8{_}4420{_}P. 241.
10095-8{_}4421{_}_Pan's Wand._--Published originally in a Christmas number of The _Illustrated London News_.
10095-8{_}4422{_}P. 249.
10095-8{_}4423{_}_A Page from the Book of Folly._--Appeared in _Temple Bar_ for 1871.
10095-8{_}4424{_}P. 282.
10095-8{_}4425{_}_The Philosopher and the Butterflies._--One of the contributions by various writers to "The New Amphion," a little book prepared for sale at the Fancy Fair got up by the students of the University of Edinburgh in 1886.
10095-8{_}4426{_}P. 294.
10095-8{_}4427{_}_The Three Palaces._--Published originally on a similar occasion to the last story, in "A Volunteer Haversack," an extensive repertory of miscellaneous contributions in prose and verse, printed and sold at Edinburgh for a benevolent purpose in 1902.
10095-8{_}4428{_}P. 300.
10095-8{_}4429{_}_New Readings in Biography._--Originally published in _The Scots Observer_ in 1889.
10095-8{_}4430{_}P. 315.
10095-8{_}4431{_}_The Poison Maid._--The author wrote this tale in entire forgetfulness of Hawthorne's "Rapaccini's Daughter," which nevertheless he had certainly read.
